Sandy structures\n\nare classified into active dunes and covered dunes and an\n\nextensive vocabulary is developed to describe sub-categories.\n\nThe descriptions are based on meteorological data (anemo\xc2\xad\nmeteicand rainfall) from local weather st-ations, ground\n\nobservations, aerial photographs and Landsat picturers. The\n\nproblem of dune reactiVation and desertification is discussed\n\nboth from the standpoint of causes and possible counter\n\nmeasures.\n\n\n17. Key Words (Selected by Author(s))\n\n18. Mainguet, L. Canon, A.M. Chapelle and M.C. Chemin\n\nRegional Physical Geography Laboratory, University of Reims,\n\nReims, France\n\nThe studiesLccrriedut during the first year\n\nof the\n\n\nDGRST [General Delegation for Scientific and Technical Research]\n\nprogram entitled "The Struggle Against the Effects of Aridity,\n\nMaradi Experimental Unit" in particular have made it possible to\n\ndescribe the locale of this experimental unit in terms of its\n\ngeororphological and lithological environment.\nSahel region, the area is exclusively sandy.\n\nSituated in the\n\nThese studies\n\n\nrevealed how much the apparent monotony of forms conceals an\n\nextreme variety of nuances.\n\nThus the second year of this program had to be devoted first\n\nof all to solting the two major difficulties which were immediately\n\napparent: (1) providing a simple description of forms and then\n\n(2) developing a vocabulary.\n\nActually, even if the topography of thessandy structures of\n\nthe Sahel environment has been scantily described, no classifications\n\nor systematic studies of the origin and dynamics of these\n\nstructures have been made.\n\nThe structures of the Sahel Niger,\n\n\nwhich are the southernmost of the Paleosahara, were positioned\n\nduring the drtest phases of the current climate.\n\nThis suggests\n\n\nthat they represent only a transformation in time and space of\n\nactive sandy structures.\n\nThus we have been led to attempt: (1) a classification of\n\nactiwe dunes and (2) a definition, description and classification\n\nof sand structures covered with vegetation and fixed in place.\n\n\n*\n\nNumbers in the margin indicate pagination in the foreign text.\n\n1\n\nThis second part was the most difficulttto do because until\n\nnow no systematic vocabulary had been proposed. We thus had to\n\nmake up an entire vocabulary. We are not entirely satisfied with\n\nit, but it represents an initial attempt. To make things easiere\n\nfor our readers we have added a glossary of the terms used.\n\nTwo classification series were made, one of active dunes and\n\none of sandy structures covered with vegetation.\n\nare\n\nbased on 5idi s\n\ni\nf-r athe& la0st\n\nThese series\n\n\nyears on the terrain \xc2\xad\n\nin northern Chad, southern Algeria and a large part of the Niger\n\nRepublic; and on photographic and satellite studies of coverings\n\nof northern Chadcxthe Niger Republic and a large part of the\n\ncentral and western Sahara.\n\nAn initial geomorphological map of\n\n\nthe entire erg of Fachi Bilma, between the Tibesti and the\n\nTermit massif was made, followed by a second map covering the\n\nHaussa erg between the Termit massif and the Ader Doutchi massif\n\nand the western piedmont of the Air. The second map relates all\n\nthe decomposition parameters of the landscapes (eolian erosion,\n\nwater erosion, effects of human occupation) to empahsize the\n\nmechanisms of breaking up and reactivation of the sandy mantle.\n\nIt then seemed essential to try to quantify the climatological /3\n\nand especially anemometrical parameters of the severe drought\n\n\nlasting from 1969 to 1973.\n\nThergfore a ggaphic analysis of the climate of the Niger in\n\ngeneral was made. This includes (1) a standard graph of rainfall\n\naverages for 12 stations during the period 1931-1960, with -- for\n\ngreater precision -- a graph of comparative rainfall for the\n\noulbi basins of Tarka, N\'Kaba and Maradi from 1956 to 1969;\n\n(2) a graph of temperature:,averages for 10 station between 1951\n\nand 1970; and (3) a graph of relative himidity in % for\' 10\n\nstations between 1961 and 1970.\n\nA special place was reserved for the study of anemometric\n\n\ndata.\n\nThis is wh \'gwe took all the meteorological measurements\n\n\nconcerning winds between 1966 and 1975 for the main stations of\n\nthe Niger.\n\nThe following wind graphs were made:\n\n(i) graphs of the average\n\n\nof predominating winds for 5 stations between 1961 and 1970;\n\n(2) monthly wind direction charts which take into account the\n\ndirection (20 directions are distinguished), the speed and the\n\nfrequency.\n\nFor these same stations the 3average frequencies\n\n\nof the major winds were also studied.\n\nAn attempt was made to bring together the wind mapping data\n\nfor all of the Niger which revealed the main directions of the\n\nwinds.\n\nSince Maradi was the only complete weather station close to\n\nthe experimental unit, it was the object of a much more detailed\n\nstudy.\n\nThis study included the following:\n\n\ni. An annual rainfall graph comparing Maradi and Tarna for\n\nthe period 1932 to 1975.\n\n2. A monthly and annual rainfall graph for Maradi for the\n\nperiod 1960 to 1975.\n\n3. A graph of maximum and minimum monthly temperature averages\n\nbetween 1970 and 1975 in Maradi.\n\n\n4. A graph of monthly percentages of humidity from 1970 to\n\n1974.\n\n5. AAtable and climatological graphs for 1975 in Maradi\n\ncovering rainfall, temperature, potential evaporation and amount\n\nof solar heat.\n\n6. Charts of the average of ground winds in Maradi for the\n\nperiod 1951 to 1960.\n\nExcept for collecting the equantitative evidence of the\n\nclimatic crisis, the study of the winds was very rushed in order\n\n\n3\n\nto allow the positioning of wind breaks.\n\nAmong the other tasks we focoused on was the study of all the /i\n\ndesertification, i.e. the breaking up of landscapes by reactivation\n\nof the sandy mantle.\n\nMore than a frontal advance of the edge of the desert, we\n\nwere able to distinguish desertification caused by human factors;\n\nspot desertification due to over~pasturing (repeated visits to\n\nspecific sites) and linear desertification along trails.\n\nAs for desertification due to physical factors -- wind, water\n\nand the combination of the two -- its location does not\n\ncorrespond to parallels of latitude. In fact, decomposition due\n\nto the effects of water preceds eolian desertification in the\n\nnorth. But the experimental unit is located in a region where\n\nwe were able to show that the eolian factors are more dagerous\n\nthan the water factors, with eolian reactivation capable of\n\nreabhing isohyet 625 mm.\n\nThe dangers due to erosion by runoff\n\n\nthere are totally insignificant.\n\nThe research method used consisted of consulting three types\n\nof data: satellite pictures, a~rial photographs and the actual\n\nterrain.\n\nWe were thus assured that each set of data -- each with\n\n\nits own scale going from 1-1,000,000 with respect to the actual\n\nsize -- included its particular information, each set differiigrf\n\nfrom the ether but all of them complimentary.\n\nTo back up the ground observations aalarge number of\n\nspecimens was collected with three key ideas in mind:\n\n1. Diagnosis with an aim to paleoclimatic investigation~of\n\nthe mechanisms responsible for the positions of the sandy material.\n\n2. Stzldy of the size of particles which are moveable at the\n\npresent time in order to see to what extent the sands of the\n\n\nexperimental unit are capable of being put into motion.\n\n3. Localization of granulometric areas in order to determine\n\nthe best areas to emphasize.\n\nFor these last studies we had to extend very widely beyond the\n\nexperimental square because by studying the material of the square\n\nitself it was not possible to understand how it got there.\n\nThe final step included:\n\n1. A mapping study of the geomorphology of the Serkin Haoussa\n\nexperimental unitkt\n\nThe map that we made on a scale of 1:20,000 by means of\n\nphotographic coverage on a scale of i:lO 000, which was specially\nordered for this study, is a document which tries to show the\n\n\n/5\n\n\nshare of morphological processes and human factors which con\xc2\xad\ntribute to changes in surface behavior.\n\n2. A mapping study on a scale of 1:1,000,000 of various degrees\n\nof the breakup of sandy landscapes. The mapping information is\n\nbased on satellite pictures and aerial photographs. The map\n\ncovering the central Niger should makb it possible to show where\n\nthb experimental unit is situated in the scale measuring the\n\nThe map of the region extending to the\n\nsouth western foot of the Air was made with the help df Unesco.\n\n\ndegrees of break-up.\n\nto thank the persons and organizations who\n\nauthorized this work, who made it physically possible, who helped\n\nus accomplish it and those who participated in it:\n\nWe are happy\n\n- the authorities of the Niger Republic, in particular\n\nMr. Garba Zakary, Director General of the INRANA [National\n\nInstitute of Agronomic Research of Niger] at Niamey, the prefect\n\nof Maradi and Mr. Sidibe Ousseini in charge of INRAN at Maradi.\n\n- The DGRST [General Delegation for Scientific and Technical\n\nResearch] Which granted us all of the necessary loans.\n\n- The GERDAT [StuAt. and Research Group for the Development\n\nof Tropical Agronomy], in particular Messrs. I.\na,\n\no,.\'"\'."\n\n4--\n\nl;n-.\n\nPveDs~:\nk\n\n-ayah\n\no\nr.-gDstic\n\n;\n\n\\Cd..\n\n..\n\n:,o\n\n\'",\n\n~\n\nV\n\n1.\'\n\n,.-/hI\n\n....\n\n. . ..\n\ni.K\n-- ,\'\n\n\\\n\n7-..\n\'\n\n.....\n\nCnI~o~\n\nkin Haussa experimental\n\n]\n\n-\n\nunit.\n\nBook an\'-\n\nActive Dunes, Covered Dunes: Definitions\n\n/7\n\n\nChapter I -- Proposal for Classifying Active Dunes\n\n1\n\nThe small-scalbhpicture of the earth provided by satellites,\n\n\nfflakes possible a new approach to eolian sand structures and land\xc2\xad\nscapes which they make up.\n\nFrom the initi &\n\nteleinterpretation\n\n\nresults it emerges that the dual topographical and dynamic\n\naspects of an eolian structure cannot be captured in\n\na single\n\n\nprocess but can only be linked together over time at various\n\nassembly levels.\n\nBefore the existence of aerial photographs\n\n\nmorphological analysis was carried out on the ground.\nstructures were first described individually.\nhardly begun when aerial coverage appeared.\n\nEolian\n\n\nThis stage had\n\nThis was\n\na new\n\n\ntool which made it possible to analyze groups of structures.\n\nAn aerial photograph by the National Geographic Institute on a\n\nscale of 1:50,000\n\nmeasuring 18 x 18 cm covered 9 x 9 km, i.e.\n\n\n81 square kilometers.\n\nThe Landsat 1 pictures brought about a new synthesis, with\n\na single picture coverig 180 x 180 km, i.e. 324,000 square\n\nkilometers.\n\n\nA final step was taken with the 1:8,000,000 readings of\n\nNoaa 3 in which the indivicual structures are no longer visible.\n\nUnderstanding them demands the study of the largest functional\n\nunits extefiding up to the entirenortherniequatorial continent\n\nfor the Sahara and 3/4 of the continent for Australia.\n\n\nThe transition from ground observations to aerial photography,\n\nthe uniting of ground observation and aerial photographs and then\n\nthe access to satellite pictures -- i.e. to a smaller and smaller\n\n1. The douements used are the following, lised in order of dee\n\n6reasing scale: (1) National Geographic Institute photographs\n\n(1:50,000); (2) Landsat 1 satellite pictures (1:1,000,000, altitude\n\n920 km); (3) Gemini satellite pictures (1:1,000,000); (4) Pictures\n\nof the Noaa 3 satellite (1:8,000,000, altitude 1,500 km).\n\n\n28\n\n\nscale -- ends up with a more and more global view, on a continental\n\nscale, which combines not only the structures but the spaces\n\nbetween structures.\n\nThe progress thus made is to restorea value\n\n\nto the "empty spaces." i.e. to the spaces where the phenomena\n\nstudied are lacking (Tables I and II).\n\nAs for the dunes, specifying them according to their location,\n\nwe were led to consider them, by degrees, first of all with\n\nrespect to the other sandy structures of the ergs, then to\n\nreintegrate them into the entire erg and finally to observe\n\ntheir development in the Sahel.\n\nWhile discovering the interdependence of the ergs in the\n\n/8\n\n\nSahara, we were led to consider them in the context of three\n\ngreat major currentg of eolian transport which sweep across the\n\nSahara between 280301 N and 130 N to the east and 160 N to the\n\n\nwest.[ 5 ].\nThe large ergs of the Sahara are arranged according to two\n\npreferential latitudes, to the north of 28\'301 N and to the\n\nN,\nSouth of 23030 \' \n between which the Sahara is poor in sandy\n\ndeposits. With pictures taken by the Noaa 3 meteorological\n\nsatellites it is possible to study the relationships between the\n\ntwo bands and to demonstrate the existence of three main sand\xc2\xad\ncarrying eolian currents. The broadest of these is in the\n\neastern Sahara, another in the western Sahara and the third\n\nfollows the Atlantic coast line from 280 to 160 N then disappears\n\nin the ocean between 200 and 160 N.\n\nThe overall direction of the first two currents is NE-SW,\n\ncloselyfollowingthat of the trade wind. They lose their\n\nhomogeneity, however, and divide into several flows while their\n\ndirections bend. It is then impossible to speak of a trade\n\nwind with respect to currents which are deflected and divided by\n\n\n9\n\nobstacles (around Tibesti), indeed even turned back (north of the\n\nAir and south of the Kreb in Naga) until they become perpindicular\n\nto the initial direction (SW of the Tibesti) over more than\n\n100 km.\n\nAlong the currents alternate sections where sometimes trans\xc2\xad\nport and corrosion prevail, sometimes accumulation. Transport\n\nprevails when the current is divided by an obstacle and\n\naccelerated afeound the same, deposit occurs when, behind an\n\nobstacle, the two currents divided by the obstacle rejoin one\n\nanother then flow together and resume the initial direction of\n\nthe trade wind. Each branch is accompanied with specific eolian\n\nstructures and a precise mode of action.\n\nIn order to avoid any ambiguity we propose a definition of\n\nthe principle terms used: erg (an Arabic term) is synonymous\n\nwith adeyen in Libya, goz in the southern Sahara, koum in\n\ncentral Asia and nafud in Arabia.\n\nFor a sandy substrate either\n\n\ncovered or not with a deflation pavement the term specifies an\n\nextended concentration of sandy structures, either homogeneous\n\nin shape or not, united or juxtaposed according to the various\n\ndegrees of organization, without any apparent organization at the\n\nedge.\n\nIt is essential to note that an erg, the main area of a deposit,\n\nis always crossed over by several transport currents, which a\n\namounts to saying that even in an erg the sand circulates. It\n\ncirculates there slowly, but sand enters the erg from one side,\n\ns\nthe upstream -- ide, and exitssfrom the other side, the downstream\n\nside. This conclusively invalidates the hypothesis that ers are\n\nwithout exception a landscape modelled by the wind from a\n\nfluviatile sandy accumulation in a basin.\n\n\n10\n\nI.\n\nDistinction Between Eolian Sandy Structures in General, Dunes\n\n/9\n\n\nin Particular and Suface Conditions of Sandy Layers\n\n1.\n\nDunes\n\n\nIn our definition of dunes we adopt the minimum height of\n\n30 cm proposed by K.W. Glennie [ 3 ], adding that there must nece\n\nnecessarily be a certain minimum slope value.\n\nA dune, in the\n\n\nstrict sense of the term, possesses an active crest, directly\n\ndue to saltationa and slippage on the slopes and due to the\n\nmeeting of two slip faces or one deposit face and another slip\n\nface.\n\nThe dunes resulttfrom a deposit in which saltation is the\n\n\nprincipal mechanism: the sand of a dune has left the ground\n\nbefore alighting on it.\n\na: Depositydunes, mobile sandy bodies in relief, involve two\n\ndifferent families: (1) some resulting exclusively from a deposit;\n\nshield; (2) other resulting from deposit and slippage along the\n\nface under the effects of the wind: barchans for example.\n\nb: Erosion dunes (Australian sand ridge), stable sandy bodies,\n\nin the origin of whibh is involved a deflation action or even\n\nerosion in a sandy mass and which are often found in conjunction\n\nwith ergs in the process bf becoming established.\n\n2.\n\nSurface Conditions: Ripples, Ripple Marks, Megaripples\n\n\n(or Zibars in Arabia)\n\nMany classifications of eolian sand structures classify\n\nripples amoqggthe dunes, regarding them as small dunes. But there\n\nare megaripples the size of dunes which can be seen in aerial\n\nphotographs at a scale of 1:50,000, and the contour of some sandy\n\nsurface conditions rises as high as or above 30 cm, but the slope\n\nis so slight,\n\ndistributed overaa distance of 15-20 cm, that -it\n\n\nbecomes almost imperceptible.\n\nIn actuallity, it is thetr topo\xc2\xad\n\ngraphy and their dynamics which make it possible to distinguish\n\nthe surface donditions of dunes properly so called.\n\nIn effect,\n\n\nthe ripples do not have an active crest and are only a superficial\n\n\n11\n\nmodification whose reptation, creep or rolling, is the main\n\nmechanism of transport of the sand which does not become dethed\xc2\xad\nfrom the ground, either because the grains are too large or\n\nbecause the wind has to overcome a slope, for example on the\n\nwindward side of a dune.\n\nA distinction has been made between barchan ripples, whose\n\n/10\n\n\nconvex side faces the wind, and linguoid ripples whose convex\n\nside polhts in the direction of the wind []J].\n\nOur classification proposal is concerned only with dunes\n\n(deposit dunes and erosion dunes).\n\nThere will be no question of\n\n\nripples or of any other surface conditions.\n\nSince geomorphological research was originated, in particular,\n\nto distinguish between shapes and their functions, with regard to\n\neolian structures it has resulted in a classification based on\n\ntwo principles: (1) the stu g of an initial module and the various\n\nassembly levels; (2) the study of the dynamics of all forms\n\ntopographically defined for the first time.\n\nClassification of Sandy Structures\n\n\nII.\n\n1.\n\nDepositwDunes\n\n\nOur classification\n\nsystem is based on the simplest form\n\n\nmaking up the primary element. Complex dunes are only combinations\n\nof this primary element, and although they vary considerably,\n\nthey can be classified on the basis of the primary module, which\n\nin classical terminology is the seif dune.\n\nThe seif constitutes\n\n\nthe first order.\n\n,\n\n\'i)\n\nSeif Dune (Saber in Arabic): First Order Dune\n\n\nThe seif is an elongated dune tapered at its downstream\n\nend with a triangular frontal section whose pointed crest generally\n\n12\n\ndescribes an arc.\n\nLinear or multidirectional combinations of\n\n\nseifs give dunes of the second order.\n\nb) The Silk Dune or Linear Dune of the Second Order\n\n(wire in Arabic)\n\nThe silk dune as a whole is a straight string of sand,\n\nwith more or less sinuous features at the microlevel due to the\n\nhomogeneous justaposition of seifs along a unique direction. The\n\nsand in silk dunes behaves as alongside a railroad track which,\n\non a surface without any topographical irregularities, could\n\ntheoretically extend to infinity by the end-to-end addition of\n\nselfs.\n\nThe width of silk dunes hardly varies no matter how long\n\nthey are, and in any event is completely independent of this\n\nlength. This confirms that the silk is indeed a structure of the\n\nsecond order, resulting\n\nfrom the succession of a certain number\n\n\nof seif type modules, maintaining its scarcely variable width,\n\nwhile the length, with respect to width, can be as much as\n\n100 to 500 times greater.\n\nIn particular, the length can be\n\n\nsubject to large variations, since it i\n\nonly a function of the\n\n\nnumber of combined seif elements.\n\nThe longest silk of the Fachi Bilma erg (mission AE 56-57\n\nNE 33 XX, photograph numbers 202, 257-258, 266-267, Merguigara\n\nE. It is 40 km long, 50-100 m\n\nsheet) passes through 19020 \' \n\nwide at the upstream end, 50 m in the center and 30-50 m at the\n\ndownstream end.\n30-150 m.\nsystems,\n\nAverage silks are 2-3 km long for a width of\n\n\nSilks are sometimes arranged in stages, in elongated\n\nwhich can attain lengths of 30-40 km (in particular\n\n\nNE 33 XX and NE 33 XXI, corresponding to the Merguigara and Ehi\n\nDohou sheets). In these stages the silks succeed each other\n\nwith brief interuptions. Drift phenomena also appear from one\n\nslik\n\nto another (&erial photographs NE 33 XX nos. 381, 382, 383).\n\n\n13\n\nIn the Fachi Bilma erg all of the silks are in festoons,\n\nsometimes without any preferential orientation of the concavities,\n\nsometimes with distinct dominant directions of the concavities,\n\nand always close to two opposite directions, north or south.\n\nLikewise the systems without preferential orientation are in\n\nfact a mixture of north orientationzand south orientation. Note\n\nthat in the most common case in which the winds have been effected\n\nby going around the Tibesti in the north, the north orientations\n\ncorrespond to concavity pointing towards the wind and the south\n\norientations correspond to a concavity pointing downwind. Silks\n\nof different concavity are not adjacent, unless at the very head\n\nof the erg in the northern branch. Neither is there any\n\ndifferent opening of the festoons to the north and to the south\n\nof the erg. However, towards the west, there appears a distinct\n\ndominant direction of silks with the concavity directed towards\n\nthe north.\n\nThe elb is a particular type of silk. It sendssout barchans\n\nleeward of the dunes. This occurs when a silk appears on a rocky\n\nsubstrate and with the resulting annual average wind\n\nmakes a\n\n\nlarge angle of 30-45.\n\n-Barehan\n\nDihedrons and Barchans\n\n\nThese are a variant of the second order (Fig. 1) whose\n\nseifs are atranged symmetrically with respect to the axis of\n\nmigration of the structure. Topographically these are crescents\n\nwith the convex side pointing into the wind.\n\nFrom a study of the localization of dihedrons and barchans\n\n(on Landsat 1 pictures) emerges the following definition of their\n\ndynamics: mobile dunes as a whole, making up supplies of sand\n\nwhich are very moveable grain by grain, which shift more slowly\n\nthan the sand of the surrounding saltation mantle. Barchans are\n\n\n14\n\ndunes which exist only on a hard substrate, for\nsubstrate.\n\nThus\n\nexample a rocky\n\n\nthey are peculiar to transport regions and\n\n/12\n\n\nthe periphery of ergs.\n\nFor barchan shapes we propose the following subdivisions:\n\n1. barchan in the strict sense of the term, original structure\n\nof the second order\n\nbecause it is composed of two symmetrically\n\n\narranged seifs on both sides of its axis of migration.\n\n2. Pre-barchan states of the cake and shield type, simple\n\nconvexities of sand without active crests.\n\n3. Barchan dihedronq the result of a barchan notch on a\n\nconvex pile of sand.\n\nThus the barchan is a complex deposit dune on the windward\n\nside and a deflation and slippage dune on the leeward side. All\n\nthree mechanisms are responsible for its dynamic transport function.\n\nThus a place should beereserved in our classification for pre\xc2\xad\nbarchan stages which do not fit into the second order.\n\nd) Ghourd or Pyramid of the Third Order with Multi-\n\nDirectional Branches\n\nThe ghourd is in\n\nfact a dominant dune, often pyramidal,\n\n\nwhich sends out silks in any direction whatsoever.\n\nGhourds correspond to nodes in a system of staionary waves\n\non which the sand is built up at the expense of the bulges, which\n\ncorrespond to sahanes.\n\nIn the case of isolated silks, the seifs as a rule are flatly\n\narranged 6n a level sandy substrate, although they can climb\n\nover obstacles.\n\nThey constitute the only abrupt relief.\n\nIn the\n\n\ncase of ghourds the silkg diverge from the top of a sandy\n\npyramid and thus are arranged with a steep slope.\n\n\n15\n\nThe seifs which form the ghourds always describe a curve\n\nwhich is more pronounced than the curves of silks. It is this\n\ntrait, even more than the convergence towards the top of a\n\npyramid, which gives ghourds their special character.\n\nWhen two\n\n\ncurved branches meet this forms the "ghorafa" or ghourd cauldrons.\n\nWhen the pyramid disappears or even changes and the seifs, which\n\nare always curved, form a growing number of cauldrons, a\n\ntransition occurs towards the draa system, also e the third\n\norder.\n\nThis transition is probably reversible.\n\nAll the transition\n\n\nforms can be seen over short distances, although at the present\n\n\ntime it is not possible to say which one derives from another in\n\nspace or succeeds it in time, assuming there is a relationship\n\nbetween them.\n\nContrary to silks, for which there are innumerable models,\n\n\nthe ghourd is a complex dune but there is very little variation\n\nfrom one to another. Thus with regard to texture the variety is\n\nto be found especially in size differences. It is on the\nstructural level that the differences are very great; ghourds\n\nare actually either arranged in semi-circles or aligned in\n\nchains of ghourds, in the first case separated by open or closed\n\n(sahane) interdune spaces and, in the second case, by coridors,\n\nor gassi, and feidj, depending on whether they are sanded over or\n\nnot.\n\nThe classification of ghourds takes these arrangements into\n\naccount.\n\ne) Groups of Dunes of the Fourth Order (Photographs\n\n6 and 7)\n\nThird order dunes are arranged in linear groups or even\n\nmore extensive groups of areas of the same behavior, such as\n\ngroups of silks, semi-circular ghourds and ghourd chains which\n\n\n16\n\n\n/13\n\n\nfor the fourth order.\n\nHere we turn our attention to groups of\n\n\nthe fourth order composed of ghourds (Fig. 3).\n\nnature of the ghourd chains in the Fachi erg\n\nshows up over a len~ht of 200 km and a width of 2 km. They can\n\nbe marked out perfectly on the Landsat pictures at a scale of\n\nThe linear\n\nl:l,000,.000.\n\nThe feature common to the structures in this first part of\n\nour classification is the mobility of the sand.\n\nThe sand has maximum mobility in the regions where transport\n\nprevails, which are opposed to regions of dominant accumulation.\n\nIn the transport sectors the sand which is displaced most rapidly\n\nis that in the saltation mantles, followed by that which is\n\nstored in the barchans. In the accumulation sectors the sand is\n\nnot totally immobile.\n\nSaltation exists, but is is restricted by\n\n\nthe roughness itself of the sandy substrate and by its\n\nsculptured relief into various dunes, e.g. silks and ghourds.\n\nIn the case of ghourd chains, the wind, which is channeled\n\ninto the gassi, sweeps across them longitudinally and pulls the\n\nsand along the ghourds. Ghourd chainsformthe transition between\n\nmobile dunes and stable sandy structures.\n\n2. Regualar Dunes or Stable Sandy Structures\n\nStanding in opposition to the mobile dunes\n\nwhich we have\n\n\njust defined and classified, are the stable sandy structures.\n\nThese are the sand ridges of the Australian geographers which make\n\nup the principle ergs\n\nin Australia.\n\nThese stable structures are\n\n\neolian structures for which the term "dune" cannot be retained\n\nonly since their relief no longer results exclusively from\n\ndeposit but from a process of even wear, i.e. erosion.\n\n\n17\n\nThey\n\ncan be defined as extensive periodic sandy waves\n\n/15\n\nseparated by longitudinal or approxiamtely longitudinal valleys.\nEven if the eolian deposits are not longgitudinal because of the\ndrift phenomena which they undergo -- phenomena which are well\nstudied in conneetion with boat wakes -- the transport and eolian\n\nerosion are longitudinal.\nA classification of stable sandy eolian structures is not\n\nconceiveable without a study of the biochimatic environment in\n\n\nwhich they appear. We have already stressed the specific\n\ncharacter of barchans in the transport regions and the specific\n\n\n-\n\n-\n\nL----\n\n-\n\n-\n\ntv\n\nr\n\n,.:\n-.:-- ,.7, , .\n-\n\ne-r\n\n-.--\n\n-,\n\nT.4\n\n-- "U,--. All of these dunes are characterized by the fact\n\nthat the sand is completely mobile.\n\nWhen the ergs are covered with vegetation, like those of\n\nthe southern region of the sahara which have become sahelian\n\nsince the last preneolithic dry phase ([illegible]), erosion forms\n\nare succeeded by deposit forms in time and space (towards the\n\nsouth).\nIn the mass of sand, which is covered with a more or\n\nless dense blanket of vegetation, the wind, armed with sand\n\nwhich it has picked up upstream in the area of active sand and\n\nwhich is the wave carrier, can carve out a system of periodic\n\nvalleys accentuated by water erosion.\n\n\n19\n\nIn contrast to mobile dunes, stabilized eolian sandy structures\n\nare characteristic of ers in the process of becoming extablished\n\nand are therefore forms which correspond to a very transient\n\nstage of incipient fixation of a sandy mass: that stage during\n\nwhich the deposited sand can no longer be moved by deflation but\n\ncan still be attacked by eolianlcorrasion. Thetool, thattis to\n\nsay the sand which is used by the wind to scui~lt the landscapes,\n\ncomes from upstream where it is still arid.\n\nHere too the\n\n\nexplanation demands a global view of the landscapes. The sectors\n\nshown in Figs. 3 and 4 succeed one another from NE to SW along\n\nthe path of the trade wind.\n\nIn many ergs transverse waves are adjacent to large longi\xc2\xad\ntudinal waves. There can even be landscapes with a checkered\n\nappearance, still perfectly visible in aerial photographs and\n\nsatellite pictures.\n\nThe former are due to a direct action of\n\n\nthe wind charged with sand and the latter are due to a cutting\n\naction whih gives the periodic alingments of the closed basins,\n\nsometimes covered with lacustrine deposits (Thar Desert, south\n\nof the Fachi Bilma erg), or continuous\n\ncorridors perpendicular\n\n\nto the wind which accentuatesthe transverse dunes resulting from\nwaves which the wind\n\ntakes charge of when it encounters\n\n\ntopographical obstacles.\n\nThese structures are studied with the\n\n\ndunes covered with vegetation.\n\nConclusion\n\nA step forward was made in the study of sandy landscapes by\n\ndistinguishing between the teiture and structure of sandy mantles.\n\nThe texture defines the parameters which make up the picture and\n\nth:\n\nstructure defines the organization of-these parameters.\n\n\nDepending on the scale of the ,documents studied, it is understood\n\nthat these two factors have differentymeanings.\n\n\n20\n\n\n/16\n\n\nThe spatial arrangement in an erg is not perceptible from the\n\nground.\n\nMan here is like an ant on a pile of sand.\n\nHe sees.the\n\n\nsand, the slopes and the primary modules up to the second order\n\nprovided that the assemblage and the distribution are not too\n\ndense nor the size too great.\n\nOrders 3, ! and 5 escape him\n\n\ncompletely.\n\nIn aerial photographs the third order (assemblage of silks.\n\nand barchans) can be perceived. The same for the fourth order,\n\nrepresented by chains of ghourds and areas covered with draa.\n\nOrders 5(ergs) and 6 (chains of ergs in a eolian current)\n\nare only within the reach of Landsat and Noaa 3 satellite pictures\n\n(Table III).\n\nThe aim of this work is to propose a classification of all\n\ncombinations, based on a simple form -- the seif -- and ending up\n\nwith compound forms which are grouped into two families, i.e.\n\nlinear families (silks) and multidirectional families (ghourds).\n\nWith this classification it is possible to assign an order to the\n\ndunes, as has been done for rivers.\n\nMobility, the fundamental parameter for any arrangement, has\n\nproved to be one of the effective criteria for classifying-dunes.\n\nIt is decreasing from the barchan to the self, to the silk and to\n\nthe ghourd and zero for erosion dunes which are smoothed out waves.\n\nIt is understood that, on the one hand, even in the barchan the\n\nsand is restrained and moves less quickly than in saltation mantles\n\nand that, on the other hand, each type of dune has an original\n\nfunction which corresponds to the prevailing context of accumulation\n\nor transport for which it is specific.\n\nFinally, in contrast to mobile dunes are stable eolian s\n\nstructures, the second large category in our classification which\n\n\n21\n\nis incomplete because we have only analyzed and classified the\n\nforms in the areas of active sand or those in the process of\n\nfixation.\n\nThe classification of eolian structures and of all the\n\n\ndegradation forms in the Sahel regions remains to be considered.\n\nChapter II.\n\nInventory of Sandy Structures on the Arid Borders\n\n/17\n\n\nof the Sahel\n\nThe Use of Photographic and Multdispectral Aerial Pictures for\n\nStudying the Active Ejtg of Fachi Bilma and Its Extension in the\n\nSahel, the Erg of Haussa.\n\nThe transition from an erg with active dunes to an\n\nerg\n\n\nwhose sandy structures are covered with vegetation was the\n\ncriterion that we used to define the border between thb Sahara\n\n)and the Sahel. Roughly it follows the southern Sahara (Niger,\n\nChad), isohyet 150 mm. 1\n\nBy analyzing Landsat 1 satellite pictures\n\n\nitias possible to determine, in a steppe with shrubs or trees,\n\nthe extension to the south of the Niger Republic and to Nigeria\n\nof a periodic topography in large waves, the origin of which is\n\npeculiar to active sandy areas.\n\nIt was also possible to\n\n\ndemarcate the quaternary wavering in the Sahara-Sahel border to\n\nthe south of the Sahara.\n\nOur reseaches have led us to propose, for the first time,\n\na classification of active eolian sandy structures [ 6 ]. Quite\n\nnaturally our interestshas been devoted to the study fof these\n\nsame structures blanketed either with steppe or savanna vegetation.\n\nThe sandy mantles currently covered by a continuous blanket\n\nof kvegetation consisting of graminaceous plants or trees were\n\npositioned during more intense dry phases than those which we\n\nknow today.\n\nTwo of these are known with certainty in the Niger\n\n\n1. According to P. Chamard, the limit of active groups in the\n\nsouthwestern Sahara corresponds to isohyet 50 mm.\n\n22\n\nRepublic: one before 2000 BP [expansion unknown] and the other\n\nat the Lower Holocene after 7,500 BP [ 2 ].\n\nP. Chamard places\n\n\nthe last rainy season of the Sahara between 10,000 and 3,500\n\nBP.\n\nDefinitions of Terms used\n\n/18\n\n\n- Covered dunes are sandy structures with a dense or open\n\nblanket of vegetation. Fozzilized [sic] by a blanket of vegetation,\n\nthe sandy structures lose their particular shape, in any case\n\ntheir ridge or peak angle and their specific dynamics.\n\n- Fixed dunes are eolian structures whose surface is paved\n\nwith coarse sandy particles which are too large to be moved by\n\nexisting winds.\n\nAround Termit in the Niger Republic these fixed\n\n\ndunes, i.e. stable with respect to eolian dynamics, are also\n\ncovered with vegetation.\n\n- A "transverse" wave is an elongated sandy eolian structure,\n\nrectilinear to slightly sinuous, with a convex profile, often\n\ndissymmetrical, sometimes with a flat\n\nsummit, most often organized\n\n\ninto a periodic Whole and whose direction is roughly perpefidicular\n\nto the dominant effective wind.\n\n- A "longitudinal" wave is an eolian structure which describes\n\na rectilinear linear path, with a convex profile, most often\n\norganized into a periodic Whole, likely to have a direction\n\nroughly equivalent to the dominant effective wind.\n\n- Reticulated waves are an assemblage resulting from the\n\ncris-crossing or superposition of the two above types of waves.\n\n- An eoloian sandy ensemble of periodic structure is a\n\nregular alternation of crests and corridors (extended interdune\n\n\n23\n\n\nhollow, sometimes the seat of ponds) following a preferential\n\norientation, sometimes longitudinal, sometimes transverse.\n\n- Monticular refers to a relief of small bulges of sand\nwith a convex profile separated by rounded hollows, arranged\nin semi-circles often following a regular network itside one\nand- the same region.\n\nWer are still not able to state with\n\ncertainty that these are eolian structures or if only the cap is\neolian.\n- Barchans and parabolas are isolated eolian structures,\n\nsometimes coalescent, but always clearly individualized, crescent\xc2\xad\nshaped with the convex side pointing to the wind wheh they are\n\nbarchans and with the concave side pointing into the wind when\n\nthey are parabolas.\n\nAt the time of their degradation when a\n\nhumid period is becoming established the barchans can give rise\n\nto perfectly conical rounded hills.\n\n\nSHilly \n\n\nrefers to nhills of sand which are slightly separate\n\n\nor coalescent.\n\nIn the Sahel environment or in any other semi\xc2\xad\n\narid tropical climatic environment involving a cover of vegetation\n\nthese hills result from the transformation of barchan type dunes.\n\nIn a study it is not possible to dissociate the Fachi Bilma\nerg from the Haussa erg because the latter is continuous with\n\nthe former in the Sahel region, which, by the way, confirms the<\n\ngranulomettic and morphoscopic studies.\n\nAt the start of our work we thought of limiting it to the\n\nstudy of the dynamics characterized by the different aspects of\n\nthe reactivation of the sandy cover and of dbflation.\n\nBut we\n\n\nhave been led to study the basis of these phenomena, i.e. the\n\ndifferent sandy accumulation relief which make up the local land\xc2\xad\nscape.\n\n24\n\n\nIn fact, two aspects of these accumulations surprised us:\n\n\n/19\n\n\n(1) the entire organization of the sandy cover and (2) the\n\ndiscovery of orginal shapes, covered sandy structures which it\n\nwas necessary to define and then classify.\n\nBy examining aerial photogrpahs wezwere able to map and\n\nlocalize these different structures, to detect the existence from\n\nnorth to southwest, i.e. from Termit to Maradi, fof a transition\n\nfrom\n\nstructures of an active erg to structures of a covered erg:\n\n\nfrom the well pronounced longitudinal and transverse structures\n\nof Termit to the indistinct monticular structure of Maradi,\n\npassing through the transverse monticular structure with the\n\nexistence of a distinct transverse structure around massifs.\n\nThus we can state that the Maradi and Tarka regions are\n\nalmost totally occupied by the slightly differentiated monticular\n\nstructure and we can note the presence of 4 other structures\n\nwhich occupy only a small percentage of the surface are.\n\nThree\n\n\nof them are not very extensive: the hilly structure, the trans\xc2\xad\nverse monticular sturcture and the longitudinal monticular\n\nstructure. The existence of the fourth structure, the longitudinal\n\nstructure, if it appears surpirsing, can be explained by the\n\nchannelization Gf one branch separated from the eolian current\n\nof the Fachi Bilma erg between the Damergou massif and th7e\n\nTegama Plateau.\n\nEach of these families of structures, depending on their,\n\ntopographical appearance, i.e. in particular their slope and the\n\ngrain size of the material of which they are composed, present\n\nvarious degrees of attraction and repulsion with respect to\n\nhuman occupation.\n\nWe also find that the region from Tanout to Zinder is\noccupied by, more differentiated monticular type structures -\xc2\xad\n\n/20\n\n\nlamost hilly -- and over smaller surface areas.\n\n\n25\n\n\nThe families of structures which occupy the\n\nlargest space\n\n\nin the region studied are the following:\n\n- separate dunes (barchans and6parabolas),\n\novoid dunes with\n\n\nclosed or open caoudeyres which indicatedthe progress of de\xc2\xad\ngradation in these structures and which can result in the hilly\n\nstructure due to coalescence, terminating with a transport\n\ncurrent, with\n\nbarchans blocked in their migration, fixed and\n\nthen degraded and regressing to the hill stage. This is proof\n\nof an abrupt incurision towards the north of a more hfmid climate.\n\n- The transverse monticular structures, which are very\n\nwidespread on the maps of Gamou and Zinder, indicate the presence\n\n-- during the transition from an active erg to a covered erg -\xc2\xad\nof a region covered by a dune structure very similar to the\n\nformation\n\nstage of the transverse structure.\n\n- The well pronounced transverse structure around the\n\n\nKoutous Massif and downwind of the Damergou Massif.\n\nThe progression which can be detected in the dune relief\n\nfrom Termit to Maradi is supported by a similar progression in\n\ngrain size and the shaping of grains of eolian origin. In fact,\n\nthe samples of sand at Termit include grains of all sizesbbetween\n\n160 and 800 Pm.\n\nAt Tanout selection has already taken place and\n\n\nthe coarsest sizes tend to disappear.\nIn Maradi the coarsest\n\ns\xc2\xb1zes have disappeared entirely.\nA selection process continues\n\nand only grains ranging in size from 160 to 315 pm exist in a\n\npreponderant manner.\n\nThe same is true for the surface conditions. The very\n\nblunted particles, iee. those whibh have undergone long eolian\n\ntransport, are distributed according to different sizes from\n\nTermit to Maradi. In Termit they still reach a size of 630 vm,\n\nin Tanout they are larger than 315 pm and at Maradi only 250 vim.\n\nThis progression in grain sizes caused us\n\n26\n\nto perceive the\n\n\nthe existence of a uniformity tying together these different\n\nregions; namely, the wind, the uniformity in the shaping of the\n\npaleorelief but even more\n\nthe dry paleoclimatic periods.\n\nThis\n\n\nwind is responsibe for transporting and sorting the material and\n\nits strength decreases along its dominant path.\n\nThis weakening\n\n\nof the wind prevented thb eolian transport of large particles\n\nto Maradi.\n\nThere is, however, a supply of coarse sand at\n\n\nMaradi which was carried by fluviatile transport.\n\nAn eolian current from the Tibesti region, which feeds the\n\n/21\n\n\nactive erg of Fachi Bilma and crosses the Termit massif .\n\npartially supplies our region with sand, shaping it with\n\ndecreasing force when our region was active.\n\nSimultaneously\n\n\nand in proportion with the deposit of grains of\n\nsand, from the\n\n\ncoarseststo the finest, it effected a load substitution and\n\ntook up autochthonous grains over the supply of sandstones ofthe\n\ncretaceous period.\n\nThus there is a triple action of the wind:\n\n\n(1) bringing in allochthon sand which is deposited selectively\n\naccording to its size; (2) load substitution, taking away the\n\nlocal decomposition material; and (3) shaping the topography of\n\nthe deposited sandy mantle. In the material deposited by this\n\neolian current is found\'afraction of particles which can be\n\npicked up again by existing winds.\n\nThe percentage of this\n\n\nfraction has increased due to the effects of soil genesis which\n\n\nthe sands have undergone.\n\nThe existence of deflation regions shaped like spearheads\n\nwhich can reach lengths of up to 200 km and which are more\n\nobvious in satellite pictures reinforces the assumption that an\n\neolian current is connected with the region whibh is capable of\n\ndepositing and shaping and also sweeping the deposited material.\n\nThis deflation action was particularly manifested during the active\n\nerg period, but it still continues to operate on a small scale\n\ntoday.\n\nThe connection between our regions and this old eolian\n\n\n27\n\ncurrent, whith was very active when the erg was active, can,\n\na priori, facilitate the resumption of eolian erosion in these\n\nregions. However, since nowadays our region is covered with\n\na blanket of vegation and fixed by a pavement of coarse material,\n\nthis resumption is normally not conceiveable without a disruption\n\nin the natural equilUibrium.\n\nThe gradual process described in these dune reliefs and in\n\nthe morphoscopy of the material making up these reliefs is found\n\nagain in the different aspects of current reactivation and de\xc2\xad\nflation.\n\nOn the maps of Gamou, East Zinder, Tanout and Zinder Magaria\n\nthere are many varied and distinct deflation and reactivation\n\nareas. Even if in places erg reactivation still takes on\n\nindistinct appearances, i.e. just beginning or still compensated\n\nfor by animal evacuations -- for example, around vilages -- we\n\noften find more distinct and pronounced reactivation in a linear\n\nshape or resembling a micro-crescent.\n\nThe maximum intensity of\n\n\nthe deflation is reached on the Gamou map.\n\nThere it appears\n\n\nin the form of shading and heavy marks alternating in successive\n\nwaves.\n\nOn the maps of Dakoro, Guidan Roumji,and Maradi and Tarka the /22\n\nsmall spots of deflation and reactivation blend together and in\n\nthe aerial photographs look like fuzzy ateas, "fuzzy features"\n\naccompanied by soft scratch marks.\n\nWhen we discovered these reactivation phenomena in the 1975\n\naerial photographs we refered back to the corresponding aeritl\n\nphotographs of 1957.\n\nAlthough the difference in scale made it\n\n\ndifficult to compare the two sets of photographs, we found thatt\n\nin the majority of cases, especially between 60 and 8\' of east\n\nlongitude, reactivation and deflation were practically nonexistent\n\nin 1957.\n\n\n28\n\nThe Sandy Structures in the Sahel Region\n\nII. \t\nA) General Characteristics\n\nThe Niger Republic (13-23\xc2\xb0N and 4-60E) :includes an -anid\n\nfringe in the north, becomes Sahelian in its interior and in the\n\nsouthern section\n\nbecomes Sudan-like.\n\nThe Sahel region maybbe\n\n\nbetween isohyets 150 mm (current limit of the active\n\ndunes) and 550 mm. va116&xc Starting upstream, this change should, by \n\n\n/31\n\nsuccessive "removals\'" be able to spread over all of the waves,\n\nbut it is likely that it became established, at least in the former\n\nincreasing ablation and the latter accumulation.\n\nThese two\n\n\nmechanisms combine and slightly defelct the waves from their\n\nlongitudinal direction.\n\nThis\n\ntight deflection made us group the\n\n\nlongitudinal waves among the doubtful forms of a fixed erg.\n\nTheir alignment close to the\'wind, their lack of active ridges,\n\nthe amount of sand affected by the phenomenon -- a sign that the\n\nwind uses its energy to scour and not to make local changes in\n\nthe shape always of the same sand -- , the scouring of valleys\n\nalong with depositing on the waves, all of this leads us to think\n\nthat the longitudinal waves are indeed a form of semi-stabilized\n\nerg, stable enough to maintain itself in the reactivated part of\n\nthewerg.\n\nThe active silks on the right tend to cause the waves to\n\ndeflect towards the north, while erosion tends to keep them in\n\na longitudinal direction.\n\nThis deflection is fundamental.\n\nWhen\n\n\nit begans to scour the wind was more or less channeled between\n\n\n40\n\nclusters and bands of silks which spread out on the corrasion\n\nvalleys and had deflected\n\nsuch that the wind then found itself\n\n\nmore channeled into these slightly deflected forms.\n\nThere is a\n\n\ncombination between the longitudinal corrasion vector and the\n\nright hand accumulation vector\n\nChange\nin Semi-circular\n\nlongitudinal transverse Monticular\n\n(longitudinal\n\nMonticular)\n\n\nThe original transverse waves can be modified into a relief /33\n\nof regulatization longitudinal waves in two contexts: (1) active\n\nergs and (2) covered ergs.\n\n1. In a context of active ers the sandy particles in the\n\nform of transverse waves are transported from bne ripple to another\n\nalong the shortest path then deposited along a right or left\n\n\n41\n\noblique line.\n\nLittle by little the transverse waves decompose,\n\n\nwhile hollows appear lined up from one ripple to another. These\n\nhollows, give rise to furrows. Between the furrows sand bridges\n\nare established from one ridge to another, giving rise to\n\nlongitudinal waves. When the transverse ridges are destroyed\n\nthey supply material which will allow the formation of longitudi\xc2\xad\nnal ridges. Thus all of thd criss-crossingstages can exist.\n\n2. When a Sahel environment replaces an arid environment\n\nthe change from transverse ripple to longitudinal ripple can be\n\ninterupted at different intermediate stages, which can be\n\nreflected in the landscape of a covered erg by different types\n\nof discontinuous longitudinal ripples.\n\nWhen the transvers wave is fixed before any degradation takes\n\nplace it can be subjected to erosion factors peculiar to the\n\nSahel: for example, the combination of primary water phenomena\n\nand winds which are still slightly active. The latter,\n\nchanneled by the transverse valleys, can help over scour the\n\nvalleys, creating a relief with a more differentiated topography.\n\nThe trnasverse wave of the Sahel is more sinuous and smaller\n\nthan in the arid regions which are not covered or only slightly\n\ncovered. This type of surface relief is not attractive to the\n\nfarmers of the Nigerian Sahel.\n\nThe transition from transverse to longitudinal is to be\n\nstudied in space and time.\n\n1. In space the currently covered erg of the Sahel region\n\nis an extension of the active F chi Bilma erg. Four types of\n\nperiodic relief succeed one another in this region, in the\n\nfollowing order going from upstream to downstream:\n\na longitudinal relief downwind from the Termit Massif;\n\na discontinuous longitudinal relief upwind from the Tanout\n\nmassif;\n\n42\n\na transverse relief which skirts th4\n\nKoutous Massif;\n\n\na discontinuous transverse relief which characterizes the\n\ndownstream portion of the covered erg until the current dies\n\nout completely at the foot of Ader Doutchi.\n\n\n2. In time, the reliefs deposited in an active erg then e\n\nevolved into existing forms when the erg became covered:\n\ncovered longitudinal wave resulting from regularization of\n\nthe transverse wave in the still active erg;\n\ndiscontinuous longitudinal\n\nwave due to regularization of\n\n/34\n\n\nthe transverse wave, halted during an intermediate stage and\n\nincomplete;\n\nfixed continuous transverse wave which only belongs to the\n\ntransverse wave oW the still active erg;\n\ndiscontinuous transverse wave inheirited from the transverse\n\nrelief in the process of formation in the still active erg,\n\nstopped in its evolution at an intermediate stage and\n\nincomplete.\n\n2. Sets of Giant Waves with a Reticulate Structure\n\n(Fig. 8)\n\n\nThe cris-cross patterns which result from the criss\xc2\xad\ncrossing or superposition\n\nof two wave types with dominant\n\n\nlongitudinal and transverse components form a landscape of mounds\n\nof sand with rather, gradual, convex slope.\n\nThe\n\nmounds are\n\n\nseparated by valleys which run in two directions: east-northeast \xc2\xad\nwest-southwest and north-northwest - south-southeast.\n\nThe\n\n\nasymmetry frequent in transverse waves is rarely found in the\n\nslopes of the\n\nmounds.\n\nThe mounds are relatively symmetrical:\n\n\nthey are always rounded as are their two orkgin&l forms.\n\nThe traditional checkering forms a three-level relief:\n\nmounds, medium levels and valleys, each level representing a\n\ntype of combination.\n\n\n43\n\n0n j\n\n+\n\nt-.\n\nCase A\n--+-raised region\n\n.\n\ni\n\n.\n\n1.4.\xc2\xad\n\n.\n\nCase B\n\n-depressed \n region\n\n\nFig. 8. This breaks them down. Their peaks are\n\nreactivatedhowever\n\nif cultivation does not take place a pro\xc2\xad\n\ntective pavement appears.\n\nBurrowing animals, which are less\n\n\nnumerous than to the north of the Koutous Massif, here too bring\n\nto the surface fine sand which can easily be moved\n\nby the wind.\n\n\nThe interdunal spaces are vast, very gently sloping faces con\xc2\xad\nverging towards miniscule temporary ponds where the material,\n\nwhich is dgy in summer, is cracked by dessication cracks when\n\nit does not bear any hoof marks. The slopes whibh lead to these\n\nponds are subjected to intense icing.\n\nIn February, 1976, (the\n\n\ntime of our visit) there existed there only a few grassy places\n\n\n54\n\nwith shrubs or trees.\n\nIntense icing and runoff phenomena which\n\n\ncan cause gullies jepordize the stability of these forms.\n\nThe experimental square consists entirely of monticular\n\nsandy structures.\n\nThis study shows its position to be downwind\n\n\nfrom all the other sandy structures.\n\nThe section of monticular\n\n\nstructures is found downstream from all other types of dunes.\n\nBook II.\n\nY44\n\n\nClimatic Study of the Niger Republic\n\nChapter I.\n\nStudy of Princip &\n\nClimatic Parameters of the Niger\n\n\nRepublic\n\n\n/47\n\n\nMonthly Rainfall dorms from 1931 to 1960 (Fig. 13)\nRainfall norms are the precipatation averages over a period\n\nof 30 years.\n\nFig. 13 shows the monthly precipatation levels for\n\n\n6 stations in the Niger Republic (Maradi is station no. 5).\n\nThe\n\n\ninset in the upper left hand corner shows the averages of the\n\nsame stations over the same period of years with the annual\n\nnumber of the days of rain.\n\nThe precipitation profilesis comparable for all the stations.\n\nIn general, the rains last from April to October, the maximum\n\nmost often coming in August (ixcept for Main6 Soroa).\n\nMaradi and Gaya hold the records for amounts of water falling\n\nper day, (259.6 and 282 mm); Bilma holds the record for the\n\nsmallest amount.\n\nBetween the two we see a decrease from north\n\n\nto south, which speeds up after 150 N, and from west to east it\n\nspeeds up after 100 E. A ADEZ\n\nFeb.\n\nJan.\n\nI267!\n\n!\n\n!\n\n288\n\n!283.\n297\n\n!\n\n278\n\n!278\n\n\n2-95\n,\n\n280\n\nI302\n\n292\n\nI\n\n289\n\n202\n\n285!\n\n282\n\n288\n\n292\n\n301\n\nthe evaporation\n\nis greatest with two maximums,=in May and\n\n\nOctober, and two minimums, in August and January at Agad~s\n\nand in December.rat Bilma.\n\nNevertheless, differences appear.\n\n\nFor Bilma the August minimum is less than the December minimum\n\n(the reverse is true for Agad s); the Bilna curve is smoother\n\nthan the Agades curve.\n\nFor August the evaporation minimums correspond to the\n\nprecipitation maximum and the timperature minimum, and for\n\nDecember-Januazyythey correspond to the single temperature\n\nminimum.\n\nFor November, the evaporations maximums correspond to an\n\ninitial temperature maximum in the dry season, and for March\n\nthey correspond to a second temperature increase in the dry\n\nseason.\n\n(The May and October shift for Bilma and Agad~s is\n\n\nexplained by the temperature curves, which are likewise shifted\n\nfor these two stations.)\n\n\n/60\n\nChpt. II. Study of Winds in the Niger Republic\n1. Prevailing Annual Directions of Winds in the Niger Repbulic\n\n(Fig. 18)\n\nThe prevailihg winds are divided into 2 regions: (1) a NNE-\n\nENE regionacorresponding to the Harmattan and a SW regions\n\ncorresponding to the monsoon.\n\n1. The Harmattan\n\nIts frequency is hardly seen to diminish as a function\n\nof latitude and longitude.\n\nTahoua receives a harmattan equivalent\n\n\not that which is received by Bilma in spite of a difference in\'\n\nlongitude of 80.\n\nBilma, N\'Guigmi and Niamey, which differ in\n\n\nlatitude by 60, receive equally frequent amounts of harmattan.\n\n\n71\n\nThis map reveals three different circulation paths for the\n\nharmattan:\n\na) the Sahara stations of Bilma and Agades receive a harmattan\n\nfrom "he NE.\n\nThis is a barely deflected continental trade wind,\n\n\nor, if there is deflection, it is due to the Tibesti massif.\n\nb) The direction 6f the harmattan which arrives at Tahoua\n\nand N\'Guigmi has a much more sautherly direction.\n\nIts NNE\n\n\ndirection results from the trade wind being channeled at Tahoua\n\nbetween north of Air and louth of Hoggar and at N\'Guigmin between\n\nnorth of Djado and the northwest point of the Tibests.\n\nc) The Sahel stations of Niamey, Birni N\'Konni, Maradi and\n\nZinder a harmattan with a much more regional tendency which is\n\naccentuated from Niamey to Zinder.\n\n2. The Winds of the Rainy Season\n\nThe monsoon decreases very distinctly and uniformly\n\nfrom Niamey to NjGuigmi.\n\nWith the exception of the Zinder station\n\n\nits direction in all places is SW.\n\nWith respect to the monsoon\n\n\nTahoua is like Niamey, but with respect to the harmattan it is\n\nlike Zinder and N\'Guigmi.\n\nOne can thus conclude that between\n\n\nMaradi and Zinder a change in the wind patters occurs.\n\nWe think that the Mayahi-Tchadaoua experimental unit has\n\n/61\n\n\nroughly the same wind characteristics as Maradi, whibh is very\n\nimportant for the installations of wind breaks.\n\nIf the installation\n\n\nof wind breaks is recommended, these will have a NW direction\n\nwhich fortunately is found to be both perpendicular to the\n\nharmattan and perpendicular to the effective wind which is not\n\ninsignificant at the beginning of the rainyseason, since the\n\nharmattan and monsoon follow roughly the same line but in\n\nopposite directions.\n\n\n72\n\n\nAI\n\nSI\n_\n\n_\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2__\n\nFig.\n\nN\n\n/\n\n18.\n\n,\not!\'\n...\n\n\'IIiO\n\n\', ,\n\n...\n,\n\ni\n\n/\n\n3"*-\n\n\n"zQ\n\n.\n\'- .\n\n\n\'\n\nPrevailing wind The Regional Tendency of the Harmattan and Monsoon\n\nAt Maradi\n\n\nHow are we to explain this deflection between N\'Guigmi\n\nand Maradi? Can it be due to the presence of the line of\n\nobstacles formed by the Air and the sedimentary haloes which\n\nextended to the south; namely, the Koutous and Tanout massifs2\n\n2. Monthly Prevailing Wind Directions in the Niger Republic\n\n/65\n\n(Figs. 19-30)\nThe study of annual prevailing wind directions in the Niger\n\nRepbulic was supplemented by a study of monthly winds. The\n\nreason for this was to provide a rigorous study for a protection\n\nprogram\n\nagainst wind erosion.\n\n\nIn January, February and March the Niger Repbulic is affected\n\nonly by winds from the NE, with the exception of Agades which\n\nreceives winds from the SE during the entire year with maximums\n\nfrom December to May. In March the westernmost stations (Niamey\n\nand Tahoua) receive the first incursion of the monsoon. This\n\ntendency asserts itself in April when the winds from the SW\n\nthen spread out through the entire Sahel region of the Niger\n\nRepblic. The monsoon still does not reach Agad~s. InMMay the\n\nharmattan dies out persisting at a longitude only up to Maradi.\n\nTahoua still receives a little of it because of its latitude.\n\nThe harmattan finally disappears. In May Agad\'s receives the\n\nfirst puffs of the monsoon which has been established.\n\nIn June\n\n\nthe above trends assert themselves: the trade wind stops at the\n\nlatitude of Zinder and the longitude of Agad~s.\n\n76\n\nNiamey and Tahoua\n\n\nreceive the strongest monsoon.\n\nIn July and August the harmattan\n\n\nreaches no further than N\'Guigmi.\n\nThe flow is from the prevailing\n\n\nSW quadrant up until September when the situation reverses itself\n\nagains with the monsoon dying out and a trade wind reappearing\n\nfurther south, at Tahoua and further west, at Zinder. In October\n\nthe situation of April seems to reproduce itself with a\n\nweakening monsoon which no longer reaches Agad~s and a trade\n\nwind which again becomes predominant, reaching Niamey.\npresents a special case.\n\nZinder\n\n\nThere is no longer any monsoon in\n\n\nOctober, while at N\'Guigmi it is still blowing.\n\nThis is probably\n\n\ndue to the direction and the existence of an obstacle.\n\nAt\n\n\nZinder the monsoon is nearly east-west which causes it to cross\n\nthe Ader Doutchi, and its crossing manages to extinguish its\n\nlasts ]uffs earlier than at N\'Guigmi which, however is further\n\naway. Because of its SW direction it does not encounter any\n\nobstacles. From November to February the prevailing trade wind\n\nblows, diminishing towards the south and west.\n\nAt Bilma, in\n\n\nDebember, winds blow from the SW which are difficult to explain.\n\nIn summary, an examination of these monthly winds allows us:\n\n1. To distinguish different stations of the Sahara as a\n\nresult of the direction of the monsoon current aid because of\n\nobstacles.\n\n2. To note a progression from east to west, from N\'Guigmi to\n\nNiamey, with a transition between Zinder and Maradi. In some\n\nrespects Tahoua is similar to Zinder and N\'Guigmi because of its\n\nlatitude.\n\nIt receives the trade wind earlier than Niamey, Birni\n\n/66\n\n\nN\'Konni and Maradi, but it keeps characteristics of a western\n\nstation in receiv&iig the monsoon earlier than Birni N\'Konni, Mar\n\nMaradi, Zinder and N\'Guigmi.\n\n3. To point o~t the transition months\'. April and October.\n\n\n77\n\n4. To\n\nsee the remarkable separation between two six-month\n\n\nseasons, from January to August and from September to December\n\n[sic].\n\n5. To better grasp the importance of the relationship\n\nbetween wind directions and the arrangement of surrounding\n\nobstacles.\n\n\n78\n\nMonthly Average of the Number of Prevailing Winds in Study of the Average of Prevailing Winds (1961-1970) \n\n(Figs. 31-35)\n\nThese monthly rose diagrams for the averages of prevailings\n\nwinds are only sketchy diagrams of the detailed rose diagrams.\n\nThey were made in connection with the work for 8 stations\n\n(5 of which are treated here). This representation actually\n\nshows only one of the three factors treated by the detailed\n\nrose diagrams, namely the prevailing direction for each month.\n\nThe speed, which is given as an average, indicated no more than\n\nmonthly fluctuations and no peaks of efficient wind, which are\n\nso significant for the planner, are shown.\n\n\n.-\n\n---- ---~-.\n\n4------v\n\n-- - - -S\n\n\nFig. 31. Agades: average of prevailing wind (1961-1970).\n\nAgad~s. The average of the prevailing winds is characterized\n\nby two opposing wind directions: (1) from the east from\n\nNovember to May then (2) from the west during the rainy\n\nseason, with two months of transition characterized by an\n\neast-southeast wind. The winds reach their maximum speed\n\nin the dry season (Jan., Feb., March). Their average annual\n\nspeed is 3.6 m/s.\n\n\n92\n\n/80\n\nb-\n\nae\n\nthe\n\nS\n\nP\n\nnrta\n\n41\n\n-\n\nt\n\n\n-\n\n1\n\n-\n\n/81\n\nI \n\n\n1\n\n1\n\nAI\n\nE\n\nFig. 32. Bilma: average prevailing wind (1961-1970)\n\nBilma. The average of the prevailing winds is characterized\n\nby a single direction, from the northeast, throughout the\n\n\nyear,\n\nexcept for June when it\n\nblows from the NO* ------- -- -------\xc2\xad\neast.\nThe\n\nhighest speeds are reached in March and April.\n\nannua\n\nThe average\n\n\nspeed is 3.89 m/s,\n\n\nI\n\nI\n\nI\xc2\xb01\n\nI\n\nFig. 33. p \'ritNE\nob-h average4- - prevailingswind (1961-1970).\n\nBirni N\'Konni.--Th-eaverage oY prvailin winds is chracterized\n\nby two opposite directions which divide the year.\n\nThis semi\xc2\xad\n\nannyal separation into two opposing winds is controlled by the\n\ndry season and the wet season.\n-\n\nAn east wind blows from October\n\n\nto March and a southwest wind from April to September. The\n\nagreatest average velocities occur in 2 different months(\n\n(February and June), each in one of the two seasons. The\n\naverage annual velocity is 2.6 rn/s with twp peak months:\n\n\n,February and June.\n\n\n93\n\nI\n\nA#\n\n\n."\n\n... .. ..\n\n-\n\nFig. 34.\nMaradi: \n\naverage of prevailing winds (1961-197o).\n\nMaradi=. The average of the prevailing winds is again 0har\xc2\xad\nacterized by 2 winds\n\nblowing in opposite directions.\n\nDuring\n\n\nthe dry season from October to February the winds are from\n\n,the east, and during the rainyyseason, from May to September,\n\nthey are from the southwest.\nHowever the transition from\n\nthe dry season to the rainy season, from March to April, is\n\nnoteworthy because of its winds from the northeast. \n The\n\nmaximum speeds \n\nare reached in January and February. In particular, we looked at (1) changes in rainfall\n\npatterns -- shortening of the rainy season or decrease in the\n\noverall annual amount -- (2) changes in wind patterns by an\n\nincrease in dry season winds, a decrease in monsoon winds or an\n\nintrusion of new winds.\n\nIt is difficult to come up with indisputable conclusions.\n\nWith respect to the climate in Africa, isn\'t it said that each\n\nyear is exceptional?\n\n\'.\n\n\nPresentation of Rose Diagrams\n\nThe rose diagrams from 1972 to 1974 were made by a computer\n\nat the University Institute of Technology of the University of\n\nReims from magnetic tapes supplied by ASECNA using a program\n\nwritten by A. It is the harmattan.\n\nDuring the rainy season winds blow from the southwest-- south\xc2\xad\nsouthwest. Here again they are easy to recognize. These are\n\nthe monsoon winds which carry water. During the summer months the\n\nmonsoon winds are accompanied by blasts of wind from the east.\n\nDuring the two interm~diate seasons, which can be defined as\n\nApril and October, winds blow from all directions.\n\nThe winds from the north=northwest affect all stations\n\nduring all months, with a maximum in the winter season from U\n\nNovember to February. We are unable to explain what causes these\n\nwinds. Likewise, we do not know the origin of the winds from the\n\nsoutheast which sometimes become dominant, as in February, 1971.\n\nIs it the trade wind from the Southern Hemisphere which crosses\n\nthe equator without being deflected, or which indeed, according\nto the scheme of G. Dhonneur, is deflected when it crosses the\nequator and becomes a monsoon, then in the most continental regions\nof Africa is deflected again so that it resumes its initial \xc2\xad\ndirection?\n\n\n108\n\nStudy of the Arithmetic Average of Prevailing Winds, Covering\n\ne\nthlieh riod of 1961 to 1970 for the Stations of Agad(s, Bilma,\n\nBirni N\'Konni, Maradi and Tahoua\n\nThis study reu&als winds which are not very effective for\n\ntransporting sand. In fact, only those of the months of January,\n\nFebruary (and March for Tahoua) have average speeds over a period\n\nof 10 years greater than 4 meters per second, i.e. winds suitable\n\nfor transport by saltation of sand when the sand is fine. The\n\nmost striking fact is, for Maradi and Birni N\'Konni, the extra\xc2\xad\nordinary similarity of direction for the average of the prevailing\n\nwinds except during the interseasonal periods of March-April and\n\nOctober. At Maradi the monsoon winds last a month longer than\n\nThus one witnesses the triumph of latitude\n\n(the difference is small, however) over the criterion of\n\ncontinentality. At Maradi the interseasonal period of March-\n\nApril is expressed in the wind pattern by harmattan-type winds\n\nat Birni N\'Konni.\n\ngiving way to winds from the east.\n\nThe interseasonal period\n\n\ndisappears completely at Birni N\'Konni, which is characterized by\n\nhaving only two clearly divided seasons -- one receiving winds\n\nfrom the east (thus a harmattan with a regional direction) from\n\nOctober to March and a monsoon coming from the southwest from\n\nApril to September.\n\nThis same well pronounced contrast is found at Tahoua. The\n\nmonths from October to April receive winds from the northeast,\n\ntypical harmattan, while the months from May to September are\n\ncharacterized by winds whose direction is specifically that of the\n\nmonsoon, however with two small shifts towards the south-southwest\n\nduring the interseasonal period: May and September.\n\nBilma is a typical SahariAn station, especially subject to\n\n/96\n\n\nharmattan 11 months out of 12. Period 1966 to 1975 \n\nBirni N\'Konni (Latitude: 13 0 48\'N\n\nLongidute: 50 15\'E)\n\n\n1 - Dry S~ason (November to March).\n\nThe dry season is clearly marked, with winds from the north\xc2\xad\nnortheast - east quadrant (1966-67-68), sometimes spreading to\n\neast-southeast.\n\n2.-\n\nInterseasonal Periods\n\n\nApril.\n\nDuring the entire month of April the winds have\n\n\nno prevailing direction.\n\nThis a typical transitiom month.\n\n\nEffective winds at this time are name.\n\nOctober.\n\nThis month can already herald dry season winds.\n\n\nIt then presents a prevailing direction from the east.\n\nThis is the\n\n\ncase dniJT966, 1971, 1973 and 1975. It can also still receive\n\nwinds of the rainy season type, but this is rare.(1969). On the\n\nwhole, October, like April, is devoid of effective wind.\n\n3\n\nRainy Season c aaIt-fipember)_\n\nFrom May to September Birni N\'Konni is under the influence\n\nof the monsoon. The effective winds can be nearly as frequent\n\nas during the dry season. The wet season months with the greatest\n\namount of effective windsaare July and August. In general, the\n\nmonsoon winds are concentrated in the south=southwest directions,\n\nwith a prevailing wind from the southwest - west-southwest. From\n\n1973 to 1975 the direction of the rainy season winds was more\n\nsoutherly with the prevailing direction spreading from west-south\xc2\xad\nwest to northwest.\n\n\n139\n\nIt\'\n\nI\n\n-t\n\nIt\n\n,,\n\n.4\n\n/\n\ntt*\n\nI\n\n-- ,\n\n,r,\n\n.1\n\n.\n\n~.\n\nA\n\n-"i\n\n140\n\nat\n\n&A17\n\n\n3\'\n\nV\n\nSS\n\n3\']2\n\n.\n\nI/128\n\nIL~--\xc2\xad\nia.\n\n*\n\n_.\n\n1 i\n\n\nAM\n\nI.\n\n,\n\n4\n\n/1\n\nI\n\n\nit\'\xc2\xad\n\n-ik\n\n"\n\n\nGAYA 1975\n\n\n141.\n\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\nI\n\nT\n\n\'\n\n:\'I\nI\n\n/\n\nf\n\n-\n\n-\xc2\xad\n\nI/K\n\n.1\n\nI\n\nI\n\n4\'"\n\n-\n\ns-\\.\n\nnt\xc2\xad\n\nIU\n\nI.\n\n- "I\'\n\n\'.\n\nS\n\nI.\n\n./\n\nAI.\n\nO\n\n96\n\n/:\'\n\n7"I\n\n-\n\n/130\n\n\'\nItt\n\n/ _\n\n\'V\n\n--\xc2\xad\n\n/\n\n/\n\n/.7.\n\nX1e\n\n\n-\n\nI\n\n\nOSA\n\n96\n\n\n/131\n\n/\n/.\n/\n\n-\n\n/\n\n/ .\n/\n\n/\n\n/\'\n\np\n\nNt%\n\n\'I\n\n&%\n\nt\n\nT\n\nS.\n\n11\n\n4\n\nU#\n\n\ntf\n\n,\n\n/132\n\n\nI.\n\n/\n\n\n1\n!/\nI\n\n,t\'\n\nII\n\n0O\n\n,i /\n\nI\n\nII\n\n\'11\n\n-\n\nI\n!\n\nI\n\nia\n\n1\n\nq\n\nI\n\nI\n\nt\n\n-\n\n984\n\n-----\n\n...\n\nU\n\n/133\n\n..-\n\n/\n\nI\n\n,\n\n/\ntt\n\nI.\n\nI\n\n-/\n\n-\n\nI\n\nI\n\n/\n\nI\n\nt\n\nI\n\n-I\n\n\nI\n\n\nI\n7,:,\n\nI\n\n-\n\n/\n\ni\nI\n\n/\n\n!\n\nMAINE SOROA"-1969\n\n\n/13\n\nI\t\n\n/\n\n/\n\nt I\n\n\nT/ \t\n\nS\n\n/t:\n\n\nI /\n\nkjl\n\n\nII\n\n\nkI \t\n\nIJ.\n\nit\n\n/\t\n\nt\n\nSI\n\n/\nI\t\n\nJ\n\n\nI\n\nt\n\nI\nII\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \t\n\nI\n\n-,\n\nI\n\n.\n\nI\n\n\nMAINE 3OR@A 1970\n\ni47\n\n..\n\n/\n\nS/\n\n,\n\n,1/\n\n/\n\n*\n\n,\n\n/\n\n-\n\n/\n\n,\n\n,,/\n\n/1\n\n_______________-..\n\n--\n\n_._______\n\n-~.S \xc2\xad\n\n---- J\n\nK-K\n\n148\n\nNIE\n\nSORA\n\n197\n\n"-\n\np\n\nr\n\n. .\n\n"\n\n\'C\n\nI\xc2\xad\n\nSF\n\n-\n\n./NE \n\n\nOOA1951g\n\n\n/136\n\nMonthly Rose Diagrams During the Period 1966 to 1975 \n\nMain4 Soroa (Latitude:130 13\'N\n\n/137\n\nLongitude: 12\'02\'E)\n\n\nFor this station we lacked data for the years 1972, 1973\n\nand 1974, but we have it for 1965.\n\n1. Dry Season (October to May).\n\nThe winds of the harmattan blow for 8 months, withaa\n\nmaximum frequency and force most often in January.\n\nIn October,\n\n\nNovember and December 1975 the winds were especially frequent\n\nand strong.\n\nThe prevailing dry season winds are strictly from\n\n\nthe north-northeast.\n\nThe transition of winds from the dry season\n\n\nto the rainy season is abrupt.\n\nIt takes place in October.\n\n\n2. Rainy Season (June to September).\n\nThe monsoon-type winds blow only for 4 months at Maine Soroa,\n\nwhich is surprising in view of the latitude of this station.\n\nThe\n\n\nsame situation occurs at Zinder, but at Maine Soroa the monsoon\n\nwinds do not have a regional direction as at Zinder.\n\nThey\n\n\nspread out over a section which varies from west-northwest to south.\n\nOne is surpirsed by the similar line followed by the harmattan\n\nand the monsoon (which blow in opposite directions),.\n\nThis\n\n\nsituation can be particularly noted on the Rose diagrams from\n\nJuly to September 1975.\n\n\n150\n\n,,-+..+\n_\n\n\n*\n\n..\n\n,.\n\n+\n. -g\n\n- \'+\ntt\n\n,\n\nisI*\n\nd\nI\n\nI\n\nI16\n\n*\n\n/139\n\n\n.2\n\n-\n\n1\n-\n\n/\nI-\n\n/\n4\n\n-\n\n*\n]-52\n\n4%\n\nU,,\n\n9\n\n.9-s\n\n/\n\n.\n\ntt\n\n.1\n\n\n.15\n\n\n,\n\nr\n\nTJ\n\nI\n\nT\n*t /\n\nA-i\n\n/\n\n,/\n\ni /i\n\nI\n\n\xc2\xa2\'P\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n4\'\'\'\n1544\n\n11\xc2\xb1\n\n/\n\n19\n\n/.\n\n,,.X,\n\n\'\'\n\nt "/ \n\n\n,"/142\n\n,//\nfl\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n/5\n\n./ "\n\na\n\nI\n\nIi\n\n*i1\n\n#\n1j\n\nI\nI\n\n-\n\n4i\n\nIC\n\n\'\n\n;\n\n,\n\n,\n\n,\n\n\'/,"\n\n...\n\nMRA~ DI 1970\n\n155\n\n1\n\n/143\n\n4\n\nd".\xc2\xad\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2e\n\n.AE3\n\nV\n\n/\n\nt4\n\nn\n\n,."\n\nl?\n\nL56\n\nMAADT 1971\n\n\nA.\n\nI\n\n?)\n\n\nA.\n\n/144\n\nI,\n\nI M.\n\n1 7\n\n4_\n\n97\n\n\\157\n\n"/\n\n/\n\n-\n\nt\n\nt,\n\nII\n\nA15\n\n/\n\n/\'J\n\n.,\n\n/145\n\n\n//\n\n//.4,\n\n\n/\n\n1581\n\n//\n\n\xc2\xad\n\n-V\n-.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.\n\n1159\n\n\n....-"J /,/\n\n\niII\n\n\n\\\n\n\xc2\xad\n\n/\n-60\n\n\ntI\'Ir\n\n97\n\n-"\n\nI\n\ns\n\n"\n\n:..\n-.\n\n\n-.\n\n.\n\n.\n\n/148\n\n-\n\n,k.\n\n.: ,\n\n\n.\n\n/,1.4\n\nN\n\n, . ? ,\n\n-,\n\n.-\xc2\xad\n\n-AA\n\n,----\'_.\n\n/\n\n,N\n\n_--*--.,\n\n.- ..\n\n"--7\n\n~\n\nA\n\n-\n\n1\n\nt41\n\nMaADl 1976\n\n16\n\nMonthly Rose Diagrams for the Period 1966 to 1975 \n\nMaradi (latitude: 13028N\n\n/149\n\nLongitude: 7\'041E)\n\n\n1 - Dry Season (November to March).\n\nFrom 1966 to 1970 the winds are distributed through a\n\nprevailing arc from 400 to 8Q0 .\n\nA shift towards the north,\n\n\ni.e. a range of wind direction from 200 to 800, is observed\n\nfrom 1972 to 1975. 1971 is an exceptional year between the\n\ntwo models.\n\nThe efficient winds (with speeds greaterrthan 4 m/s), which\n\nare sand-carrying winds capable of causing reactivation, are\n\nmost frequent in the dry\n\nseason.\n\nDuring the period 1966 to\n\n\n1970 they are concentrated in a prevailing arc from 200 to 400,\n\nand between 1972 and 1975 they are concentrated between 40\n600.\n\nand\n\n\nThe same expeptional situaton is observed in 1971 when\n\n\nthe direction of efficient winds of maximum frequency is 1400.\n\nThe efficient winds are most frequent in November and December.\n\n2 - Interseasonal periods\n\nApril: there is no prevailing wind, except if there is an\n\nextension of the three preceding months.\n\nThis was the case in\n\n\n1970-71 and 1973 when the dry season was extended up until May.\n\nAlso at this time there are the most effective winds: their\n\nfrequency always increases in proportion with the frequency of winds\n\nin general.\n\nMay: First barely perceptible signs of a prevailing wind\n\nsector becoming established between 180\n\nand 260\'.\n\n\nOctober: transition month between the rainy season and dry\n\n\n162\n\nseasdn, can already be either already under the iifluence of\n\nprevailing wdnds of the rainy season or maybbe without any pre-1\n\nvailing wind.\n\nBut the winds of October can also extend the\n\n\ndry season and thus still receive winds coming from an arc\n\nranging between east and northeast.\n\n3 - Rainy Season (June to September, October for 1969).\n\nThe winds are concentrated in a clearly dominant sector\n\nfrom the south, from 2000 to 2600, and 2800 maximum.\n\nThere\n\n\nare very few efficient winds, and these always come from between\n\n2200 and 24\'60.\n\nThe winds from the southeast, which are still unexplained,\n\nexist in all seasons (February 1971, October,1975, May-June 1976).\n\nHowever, they are interesting since when they do exist they are\n\nefficinet and dominant.\n\n\n163\n\nI\n\n/\n,\n\n/\n\nS\n\n/150\n\n-\n\n"\n\nI\n\n/\n\n\\,\n-I\n\nl\n\n-- - Dry Season (October\n\n-\n\nNovember to March - April).\n\nThe prevailing winds are concentrated in a distinct arc\n\nranging from north to east.\n\nIn the course of the 10 yearse\n\n\nstudied 3 prevailing directions appear:\n\n(,1) winds with a zonal\n\n\ndirection from 1966 to 1968, (2) winds from the north in 1969\xc2\xad\n1972 and (3) from the northeast between 1973 and 1975.\n\n2 - Transition months.\n\nApril. The winds sometimes extend the tendency of the dry\n\nseason, i.e. ranging in an arc between north and east-northeast\n\n(1970-72), or else they come from all directions possible, with\n\na greater frequency from an arc ranging between northwest to\n\nnorth.\n\nOctober.\n\nNo direction dominates, except when Octoberris\n\n\nalready under the influence of dry season winds, which is frequently\n\nthe case (1970 to 1973 and 1975).\n\n3 - Rainy Season (May to September).\n\nwill be seen that at N\'Guigmi there is no clear cut\n\nestablishment of winds from the sector corresponding to the mon\xc2\xad\nIt\n\nsoon.\n\nIn 1973 the monsoon does not appeart\n\nThe entire year was\n\n\nsubject to the effect Of winds from the northeast. \n\nLongitude: 5\'15\'E)\n\n\n1 - Dry Season (October to March),\n\nThbhing the dry season Tahoua is a station typically under the\n\ninfluence ofithe harmattan from the northeast, except in Tahoua.\n\n\n198\n\n3 - Rainy Season (May to September).\n\nIf one observes the wind parameter, one is stuck by the\n\nabruptness of the incursion of the monsoon which replaces the\n\nharmattan
