Rose Diagrams for the Period 1966 to 1975\nZinder (Latitude: 130 48\'N\n\n/196\n\n\nLongitude: 91 0 0\'E)\n\n\n1 - Dry Season (November to April4.\n\nThis season extends from November to April from 1968 to 1973\n\ninclusive.\n\nIn 1966, during March and April, prevAiling winds blew\n\n\nfrom the northwest.\n\nIn 1973 the dry season winds continued up\n\n\nuntil May. The dry season begins in October, except in 1969 when\n\nit begins in November. On the whotke the winds Come from the\n\nnorth-northeast, sometimes from the east-southeast.\n\nThe dry\n\n\nseason is perfectly marked at Zinder with frequent and effective\n\nprevailing winds of the harmattan type, especially during January\n\nand February.\n\n2 - Interseasonal Periods\n\nApril-May.\n\nApril can no longer be considered as the only\n\n\ntransition month.\nseason.\n\nBoth April and\n\nMay constitute the transition\xc2\xad\n\nThe incursion of the monsoon made itself felt only in\n\n\nMan and only in 1968, 1970-72 and 1975.\n\nOctober.\n\nOctober is clearly the first month of the dry\n\n\nseason, with winds already of the harmattan type, except in October,\n\n1969, when winds from the northwest were dominant.\n\n3 - Rainy Season (June to September).\n\nIn June, on the whole, the direction of the monsoon is remark\xc2\xad\nably zonal and persists in July, August and September during the\n\nyears 1966 to 1971. Starting tn 1972 the direction is the usual one\n\nfrom the southwest, but it resumes a zonal direction in 1975.\n\nThese observations are particular for Zinder.\n\n\n212\n\n\n7.\n\nAnalysis of the direction and Average Frequency of Monthly\n\nAverage Prevailing Winds.\n\n/197\n\n\nComparative Study of Interannual\n\n\nInstability Indicies of the Winds.\n\nEach station is studied with respect to two specific wind\n\nvariables: frequency and direction.\n\nThe study is based on: (1) meteorological data supplied for\n\neach station (8\n\nreadings per day) for the years 1966 to 1975 and\n\n\n(2) on rose diagrams obtained by processing this data statistically.\n\nThe frequency is defined by the number of readings for a\n\nprevailing direction for one month, and the frequency percentage is\n\ndefined by the number of readings for a dominant direction for one\n\nmonth with respect to 240 rreadings (= 30 x 8 per day).\n\nBy using a graphical representation of\n\nthe average\n\n\nfrequencies of average prevailing winds and by means of a composite\n\nrepresentation of the monthly average variations of the period of\n\nyears considered, the intended aim is to establish the main pre\xc2\xad\nvailing direction of the winds and for each of these directions\n\nto isolate both their direction and frequency components.\n\nA second more refined study will be made using the same\n\nmethods on the effective winds.\n\nWe define our terms as follows:\n\n- monthly average prevailing wind: the average angle -\xc2\xad\nobtained from data for the years 1966 to 1975 -- of directions\n\nwhose frequency is the greatest for the month considered.\n\n-\n\ndispersion index: the ratio\n\n.\n\nabsolute deviation from the mean\nArithmetic mean\n\n\nx100.\n\nThe absolute deviation from the mean is the sum of deviations from\n\n\n212\n\n\nthe mean in absolute value of each term of the series divided by\n\nthe number of terms.\n\n- instability index: the product of frequency dispersion\n\nindicies and direction dispersion indicies divided by the monthly\n\naverage frequency of the st&taion considered.\n\nComparative Study of Interannual Instability Indicies of the\nWinds of the Stations in the Niger Repbulic Studied\n\n\n/198\n\n\nFrom a comparative study of instability indicies of the winds\n\nwe can distinguish two groups of stations: (1) those having a\n\nsingle instability maximum (Niamey and N\'Guigmi) and C2) those\n\nhaving two instability maximums (Tahoua, Zinder, Maradi\n\nand\n\n\nBirni N\'Konni).\n\n1) The ,Group of Stations with One Instability Maximum\n\nThis graup is made up of two stations whose interannual\n\nvariations for the seties of 10 years under consideration are not\n\nThis reflects the fact that they are located at\n\nthe two ends of the Sahel region of the Niger Republic. N\'Guigmi\n\nhas very Saharian features, such as a large variation in rainfall\n\n\nhomogeneous.\n\nand one year in the series without any south or southwest wind.\n\nOn the contrary, Naamey shows a high degree\n\n(wet season wind).\nof uniformity from year to year. The month of march, whose index\n\nis the highest, has all the characteristics of a transition month,\n\nhence a beginning of the wet season as early as march, a month\n\nwhich already receives periodic rainfall.\n\n2) The Group of Stations with Two Instability Maximums\n\nThis group is divided into two subgroups:\n\nconsists of the stations whose instability\n\nmaximum is situated at the end of the wet season (Octobrer): Birni\n\na) one subgroup\n\nN 1 Konni and Maradi.\n\n\n214\n\n\nThese two stations thus have a wet season whose instability\n\ntends to prolong the potential period of precipitation.\n\n\nb) This group consists of the stations whose instability\n\nmaximum is situated at the beginning of the wet season (April):\n\nTahoua and Zinder.\n\nThis type of instability characterize6 stations whose wet\n\nseason ends during the month of October and begins some time in\n\nApril.\n\nThese two stations therefore have a wet season whose instability\n\ntends to reduce the potential period of precipitation\n\nThe study of this instability index is supported by the\n\n/199\n\n\nanalysis of Jo\xc2\xa7l Charre, who states that "one can, however,\n\ndeduce that the duration of the wet season has all chances of\n\nbeing as much longer as the beginning was early."\n\nIn addition, it should be noted that the presence of dry\n\nyears (1969 to 1972) in the series does not play a particularly\n\nsignificant role in the distribution of the types of months\n\n(with winds prevailing from the east or from the south-southwest)\n\nover all of the months of the year, i.e. not forfthe directions\n\nbut for the wind intensities.\n\nThis comparative study reveals the break in the rainfall\n\npattern between Maradi and Zinder. Maradi represents the eastern\n\nmost Sahel type, which is characterized most homogeneously-by\n\nNiamey. Zinder represents the westernmost station of the Sahara\n\ntype, which is characterized by N\'Guigmi and Bilma. Tahoua is\n\nfound in this region because of its latitude and the obstacle effect\n\n\n215\n\n\nThe break is also manifested by the relative values\n\nof the instability index maximums. Maradi has the highest values\n\nof the Sahel type.\n\nof the Air.\n\nThis leads to the following fundamental conclusion: the means\n\nof the instability indicies of the wind decrease from N\'Guigmi\n\nto Zinder then, after a break region, they undergo a similar\n\ndecrease from Maradi to Niamey.\n\nAgad~s.\n\nAnalysis of\n\nthe Prevailing Winds and Their Frequency\n\n1966-1975 (Fig. 46).\n\nPrevailing Winds\n\nThe prevailing winds are characterized by the absence of\n\na monthly dominance of winds from the sector ranging from south to\n\nsouthwest. A group of months in which the average of the\n\nfrequencies is very stable. From May to August, these are the months\n\nof the wet season.\n\n2. The months of high frequency: November, December and\n\nJanuary. These are the months whose dominant winds are directional\n\nand not sectorial. They reflect the stability of the dry season\n\nwinds.\n\n3. The months of low frequency.\n\nThese represent the\n\n\nmonths of April and October. They are the transition months. This\n\nconfirms the fact that the transition for a station is characterized\n\nmore by a dominant range of winds than by a dominant direction.\n\nInstability Index\n\nThree periods of the year can be differentiated on the\n\nbasis 6f the instability index:\n\n1. The months with a very low instability index from\n\n2.43 to 3.54, from June to September.\n\n/205\n\n2. The months with a high index, April and October.\nThese are the transition months. It should be noted that April\n\nis bounded by two months with a rather high degree\'of variability\n\n(March and May), which suggests that the transition period is\n\n\n221\n\n\nlonger than the frequencies and directions at first seem\n\nto\n\n\nindicate.\n\n3. The months with an average index. These are the\n\nmonths of the dry season. It should be noted that the instability\n\nindex is smaller in January and February. From this it is possible\n\nto conclude that January and February are two months whose\n\ndominant winds are the most stable of the dry season.\n\nConclusion\n\nBirni N\'Konni is a station which corresponds to a\n\nregion with a low instability index for the rainy season months,\n\na high instability index for the transition months and an average\n\nindex for the months of the dry season. The maximum instability\n\nis found at the end of the rainy season.\n\nMaradi.\n\nAnalysis of Prevailing winds and Their Frequency.\n\n/207\n\n\n1966-1975 (Fig. 18).\n\nPrevailing Winds\n\nThree groups can be distinguished:\n\n1. March and April, which\n\nare the transition months.\n\nConclusion\n\nOn the basis of the instability index the station is\n\ncharacterized by a high degree of interannual stability and three\n\ngroups of months can be distinguished:\n\n(1) the rainy seasonffrom\n\n\nMay to October, (2) the dry season from October to February\n\nand\n\n\n(3) the transition months of March and April.\n\nThe Niamey station,lef all the stations studied, is most\n\ncharacteristic of the annual reversal of prevailing winds.\n\nN\'Guigmi.\n\nAnalysis of Prevailing Winds and Their Frequency.\n\n/213\n\n\n1966-1975 (Fig. 50).\n\nPrevailing Winds\n\nThe prevailing winds of N\'Guigmi are characterized by\n\na high degree of heterogeneity.\n\nFor all of the months\n\nthe\n\n\ndispersion index is very high (never less than 14.67 for August).\n\nThis large variation is characterized by the presence of an\n\neasterly orientation for all of the months of the year.with\n\nrespect to average winds (1973 does not have any prevailing winds\n\nfrom the\n\nsouth or southwest).\n\n\nThreee groups of months stand out:\n\n1. of years under consideration.\nJanuary and from June to September.\n\nThese are the months of\n\nThere is also a group of\n\n\nmonths with an average instability index (February, March, May "character" of the two periods.\n\nObtaining a Monthly Instability Index\n\nThis index is obtained by multiplying the dispersion\n\nindices of frequencies and dominant directions and then dividing\n\nthis product by the average frequency of the month for the years\n\nunder consideration. This makes it possible to reduce thb\n\ndifferences of the two dispersion indicies, while maintaining a\n\nproportionality which highlights the differences (in absolute\n\nvalue).\n\n\n235\n\nTahoua.\n\nMonthly Stability Index \n\n\nJanuary\n\n/218\n\nMarch\n\nApril\n\n4.07\n\n21.21\n\n10.56\n\n105.76 17.83\n\n10\n\nIs\n\nFebruary\n5\n\n7\n\nCategory\n\nJuly\n\nIS\n\nAugusu\n\nSeptember\n\nMay\n\n1\n\nJune\n\n\n4.60\n\n\n6\n\n9\n\n\nOctober\n\nNovember\n\nDecember\n\n\n3.79\n\nCategory\n\n3.03\n\n5.10\n\n91.18\n\n36.43\n\n21.30\n\n\n11\n\n12\n\n8\n\n2\n\n3\n\n4\n\n\nIS = Stability index\n\nIdF = Study of dispersion, frequency\n\nIdD = Study of dispersion, dominant direction\n\nDm = Average dominant direction\n\nZinder.\n\nAnalysis of Prevailing Winds and Their Frequency.\n\n/220\n\n\n1966-1975 (Fig. 52)\n\nPrevailing Winds\n\nOn the basis Wf dominant directions from year to year\n\ntwo groups of months\n\ncan be distinguished:\n\n\n1. The different years differ strongly from the overall\n\naverage during the periods from 1932 to 1949 afid from 1967 to\n\n1976.\n\nBut they differ in different ways.\n\nIn the fadta tperiod\n\nu\n\n\nthe differences are strong in two directions (they cancel each\n\nother out).\n\nIn the second period the differences are strong in\n\n\none direction (negative).\n\n3. The difference from the periodic average of the years\n\nmaking up this average.\n\nIn the ffrst period the years 1939 and\n\n\n1949 differ the most from the average, by +7.7 and -5 respectively.\n\nIn the second period 1950 and 1959 differ\' the most from the\n\naverage, by +4.5 and -2.5 respectively.\n\nIn the third period the\n\n\nyears which differethe most are 1969-1972, with a peak in 1969\n\nof +4 and a minimum in 1972 of -3.\n\nMonthly Rainfall at Maradi from 1960 t.o1975 (Fig; 54).\nThe maximum rainfall 6ccurs the months\nhowever there are very large differences\n\n/228\n\n\nof July and August,\n\n\nfrom year to year,\n\n\nwith more than two fold differences for July and more than\n\nthree\n\n\nfold differences for August.\n\nIn general, April receives only traces of precipitation.\n\nWhen\n\n\nthe April precipitation amounts to more than 30 mm they do not\n\nnecessari3iy indicate the beginning of a rainy year.\nin\n\nFor example,\n\n\n1966 37.8 mm of rain fell in 15 days in-April out of an\n\n\nannual total of 631.7 mm, and in 1968 36.9 mm fell in 4 dAys out\n\nof an annual total of 362.2 mm.\n\nOctober ends the rainy season.\n\nWhen the\n\nthrough this month, the year is a rainyy one.\n\nrainy season extends\n\nHowever there are\n\n\nrainy years without any rain in October.\n\nThere is no direct relationshibp between high precipitation\n\n\n242\n\nlevels and the number of days of rain recorded. mm\n\nSurface Wind at Maradi from 1951 to 1960 (Fig. 58).\n\n/235\n\n\n1. Dry Season\n\nOut of a total of 100 observations 56 observations of\n\nwind were made and 44 of calm.\n\nThe wind from the east is largely\n\n\ndominant, with 35 observations, followed by the wind from the\n\nnortheast with 13 observatibns, and finally the wind from the\n\nsoutheast with nearly 5 observations. The winds from the north,\n\nnorthwest, west and southwest altogether represent only 3\n\nobservations.\n\n\n247\n\n/234\n\n<-<\n\n\\\n\n\\,\n\n\n\\\n\n/\n\n-A.\\\n\n\\\n\n------\n\nRa\n\nFig. 58.\n______Dry\n\n-----\n\nn\n\n-I\n\nea\n\no \n\n\nc\n\nlm\n\n=\n\n3\n\n%\n\n\nSurface wind at Maradi (1951-1960)\nseason (calm\n44%)\nRainy season (calm\n\n=\n\n30%)\n\n2cm = 5%\n\n2. Rainy Season \n\nOut of a total of 100 observations, 62 observations\n\nof wind were made and 38 of calm. The winds from the west and\n\nsouthwest dominate, with 23 observations apiece, followed by the\n\nwinds from the northwest with 6.5 observations, and finally the\n\nwinds from the south with 5.5 observations. The winds from the\n\n\n248\n\n/235\n\nsoutheast, east, north and northeast altngether_represent only\n\n4 observations.\n\nThis graph has the advantage of showing the directions of\n\nthe dominant wind per season. Nevertheless, this representation\n\nwipes out the directions of secondary wind, the importance of\n\nwhich is recognized however, as this graph gives the impression\n\nthat the number of\n\ndays of wind is greater in the dry season\n\n\nthan in the rainy season, whereas in reality the reverse is\n\ntrue. Moreover, it is regretable that the speed of the winds\n\nis not indicated.\n\nFinally, does the calm signify an absolute\n\n\ncalm of winds or winds of low velocity which are thus ineffective?\n\nThe dry season winds from the northeast-east sector clearly\n\nmatch the harmattan and reflect the zonal bending that the\n\nharmattan undergoes in the Nigerian Sahel.\n\nThe rainy season\n\n\nwinds from the southwest-west sector result from the incursion\n\n8f the\n\nmonsoon and conform to the familiar plan.\n\n\nBook III. The Experimental Unit of SerkinliHaussa\n\n/236\n\n\nChpt.Cih. Analysis of the Serkin Haussa Experimental Unit Based\n\n/238\n\n\non Landsat 1 Satellite Pictures (Fig. 59)\n\n1. Discussion of the 1:20,000 Map of the Serkin Haussa \n\nExperimental Unit (Separate sheet)\n\nReactivation Mechanismssfor the Sandy Mantle by Wind\n\nand Water Erosion and\nThe 1:20,000 map\n\nof\n\nthe Effects of Human Occupation\n\nthe\n\nexperimental unit of Serkin\n\n\nHaussa (Niger Republic) was basically done to evaluate the\n\nreactivation mechanisms of the sandy mantle due toowind and\n\nwater erosion\n\nand to evaluate the effects of human occupation.\n\n\nIt was made from the special coverage of aerial photographs\n\n(1:10,000) taken by the French National Geographic Institute in\n\nOn this map it was attempted to make an inventory of the\n\norganization of different elements of the physical environment\n\n1976.\n\nand of the main parameters responsible for the breakdown of land\xc2\xad\nscapes.\n\nThese parameters are grouped into two categories:\n\n\n(1) borphological processes, among which we can distinguish\n\neolian reactivation either by an overloading of sand or by\n\ndeflation and water erosion, (2) human factors leading to a change\n\nin surface behavior.\n\nI. Surface State @f the Physical Environment\n\nThe sandy monticules and interdunal valleys were care\xc2\xad\n\n257\n\nIt was our htope,,by using a mapping format, to be\n\n\nfully mapped.\n\nable to discern an organization which we could not find by simply\n\nobserving aerial photographs and by making ground observations.\n\nBut the few rare tendencies which we were able to find are areas\n\nwhere the monticules are more numerous and, on average, separated\n\nby\n\nbstances of from 80 to 120 meters, and other areas where the\n\n\nmonticules are almost nonexistent, as is the case east of the\n\nvillage of Azazala in an extended region whereaall of the runoff\n\nseems to converge. The same is true south and northeast of\n\nSerkin Haussa.\n\nIt seems that outside of these three bands thermonticular\n\nhas densities roughly equivalent to the rest of the map. The\n\ngreatest density is located east of the village of Guidan Angb,\n\nfollowing a\n\nnorth-south line.\n\n\nThe interdunal valleys were differentiated from more or less\n\ntemporarily flooded depressions. We indexed 9 tabkis, which are\n\nnatural ponds often managed by man. Reactivation by Eolian Deflation Processes\n\nWe have distinguished several types of deflation\n\n(1) area\n\n\ndeflation, i.e. in more or less extensive irregular areas; (Z)) ra\xc2\xad\n\n260\n\nter deflatiop, i.e. describing a dented topography when looked at\n\nin detail (dents or craters on the order of decimeters); and\n\nfinally (3) curious periodic or spot reactivation tendencies.\n\nThere is a distinct contrast between regions where water\n\nerosion predominates and regions where wind erosion predominates.\n\nWhy this contrast? Experimental Unit: The Village and Its Surrounding Rings\nof Agriculture; the Means of Cultivation; the Notion of a\n\n\n/247\n\n\nMobile Sandy Covering\n\nAround the villages the agricultural soil is laid out in two\n\nor three rdhfgs.\n\nTheifirst, immediately around the village, is\n\n\ngenerally cultivated in sorghum and corn, which, because they are\n\nmore demanding than millet, benefit from manure.\n\nThe addition to\n\n\nthe soil of cattle manure makes an excellent protection against\n\nwind erosion. The natives are very conscious of the fact that the\n\nmanure compensates for deflation when the latter reaches too high\n\na level close to the villages.\nappears\n\nIn the 4erial photographs there\n\n\na form of risible deflation south of\n\nthe village of\n\n\nGuidan Gouge like a smaltlepro6syipf slightly depressed round\n\nareas in whibh the covering of vegetation is destroyed.\n\nAt\n\n\nGuidan Goug&\'this degradation was compensated for by a manure of\n\ncamel dung and a covering of twigs.\n\nThe second ring, further out, is planted with millet which is\n\ngenerally harvested in October, the beginning of the dry season\n\nwhen the harmattan blgins to blow.\n\nAt Maradi, where the harmattan\n\n\nblows, on average, from October to February inclusive,, the winds\n\nfrom the east are strong and the millet, once harvested, no\n\nlonger provides a protective covering. In general, it is in this\n\nsecond ring cultivated in millet, sometimes also planted with .\n\n"nieb6," that the dangers of wind erosion are greatest. These are\n\n\n263\n\n\nincreased by an 2imroverishment of the soils around certain\n\nvillages due to 20 consecutive years of cultivation without ever\n\nletting the land lie fallow, which seems surprising in an\n\nespecially quartzy sandy material.\n\nSeveral inquiries among the peasants made us aware of a notion\n\nwhich had escaped us during the first year of our work: the notion\n\nof a mobile sandy covering.\n\nIn fact, good ground planted with\n\n\nmillet is a very mobile sandy graund.\n\nThe red clastic dunes and\n\n\nthe interdunal spaces richer in muddy material are considered by\n\nthe natives to be much less favorable to the cultivatdon of millet.\n\nWhen the cultivation of crops &xtends for too many years the\n\nmobile sandy covering can be completely carried away by\ndeflation and the B horizons crop out.\n\neolian\n\n\nThen, in order to avoid a\n\n\ncatastrophic drop in production, the natives have recourse to\n\nletting the land lie fallow, allowing the mobile sandy cover to\n\nreform.\n\nThus, southwest of Guidan Lali Bakan4, a very hard region,\n\n\nwhere the B horizon crops out, was left fallow for three years\n\nwhile waiting for the qand carried by the wind and trapped by the\n\n/248\n\n\ngraminaceous covering to become thickneneugh to again allow\n\nprofitable cultivation of millet.\n\nThe peasants appreciate the\n\n\nsand brought in by the wind ("good for theecultivation of millet"),\n\nbut, on the other hand, they fear deflation which causes thb\n\nelastic sandy substrate to crop out.\n\nThe mobile sandy covering favors keeping things moist.\n\nIt is\n\n\nunder this mobile sandy covering that the chances of finding mois\xc2\xad\nture at a shallow depth are maximum during the dry season. When\n\nthere is no covering of mobile sand the cultivation of millet is\n\nonly profitable in years of heavy precipitation.\n\nThe thickness of the mobile sandy covering varies: 5 cm near\n\nthe The Dangers of Reactivation by Water Enosion in the\n\nExperimental Unit and the Area Upwind of the Unit\n\n\nscapes.\n\nNiger Republic."\n\nerosion (ratio between the length\n\n2\nof rill marks in km and the surface area in km ) is very low in\n\nthe experimental unit. Thus the dangers of reactivation by water\n\nmechanisms there are negligible, in contrast to what is bccuring\n\naround 150 501N, i.e. approximately at the latitude of the southern\n\nThe index of runoff\n\ntip of the Termit Massif where a band of gullred dunes is observed\n\nwhich stops around 1410\'N.\n\nOn the basis of a combined study of wind and water actions\n\neast and south of the Termit Massif made it possible to locate\n\na border between a sandy landscape with typical arid forms and\n\na landscape of sandy structures degraded by Sah&Tian mechanisms,\n\nsuch as the effacing of dunes and gullying. The band of gullied\n\ndunes forms a true transition between the two landscapes. In this\n\nregion the precipitation levels (from 250 mm to 400mmm in the south)\n\nare sufficient to cause runoff and the dovering of vegetation\n\nis\n\ninsufficient to counteract it.\n\n\narea are-- of two sorts: old rounded gullies\nand more distinct gullies, probably more recent. These gullies\naffect two kinds of covered eolian structures: hilly and transverse\nThe _gulliesin this\n\nstructures.\n\n266\n\n\nThe\'map of this band (1:1,000,000 scale map) and\n\nits comparison with the isohyet map show that water erosion on\n\nsandy mantle is less effective with precipitation levels\n\nof 600 mm than with from 250 to M00 mm of precipitation.\n\n\nthe\n\nIn the experimentAl unit water erosion takes place only in\n\npreferential locations governed by pedological factors of dif\xc2\xad\nferentiation.\n\nIn fact, the gullies only appear=in areas, or on\n\n\nthe periphery of areas, with a subsoil of lateritic gravel or in\xc2\xad\ncipient hardpan, and on reddened dunras, thus with a slightly\n\nelastic mass, when they are subject to "glazing" as a result of\n\noveruse which has caused deflation of their mobile sandy mantle.\n\nThis glazing promotes the start of in-tensaeunotff\nThus on the\n\nbasis of the postion of areas with lateritic ggavel or of red\n\ndunes it is possible to easily predict water erosion dangers in\n\ndifferent regions of the experimental unit.\n\nHowever, the danger\n\n\nof water eetosion is still not very high.\n\n2.\n\n2. Wind Reactivation\n\n/251\n\n\nThere are four wind reactivation mechanisms: (1) up\xc2\xad\nrooting,((2) erosion, (3) sprinkling With sand and avalanche\n\neffects, and (4) buildup of small structures such as nebkas or\n\nsilks.\n\nThe\n\nreactivation signs o~served differ according to the\n\n\nscale of the documents referred to.\n\nA. Diagnosis of Reactivation Using Satellite\n\nPictures\n\nThe following can be detected on the 1:1,000,000\n\nscale ERTS pictures:\n\n1. Circalar deflation: areas of reactivation onomonticular,\n\nlongitudinal monticular and transverse structures.\n\nA structure\n\n\n267\n\n\nof central white areas, 2-4 km in diameter, sometimes coalescent,\n\nshows up particularly cblearly on channel 7.\n\nThe black central\n\n\npoint corresponds to the village and the surrounding ring of\n\nfertilized ground. The white ring surrounding the village cor\xc2\xad\nresponds to cultivated agricultrual ground.\n\nThus the reactivation\n\n\nconnected with the villages and agricultural soils can be\n\ndetected on satellite pictures.\n\n2. Longitudinal deflation: reactivation occurs\n\nalong the\n\n\nstreaks, but in a discontinuous way whibh makes it difficult to\n\ndetect on\n\n1:50,000 scale aerial photographs and easier to detect\n\n\non satellite pictures.\n\n3. Two types of eresion._(.)\n\nin stria on the dunal structures,\n\n\nsometimes arising downwind from an obstacle, or (2) in sweeping\n\nbands downwind\n\nfrom the Koutous and Tanout-imassifs.\n\nThe sweeping\n\n\nbards often describe an arc of a circle, while the erosion stria\n\nare rectilinear.\n\nTheir edges, which are always sharply defined on\n\n\nthe northside and wavy on the southside, are dissymmetrical.\n\nThe small sweeping bands are shaped (in top view) like a spearhead\n\ntapering downwind, whereas the large bands taper without reaching\n\na perfect spearhead shape.\n\nOn the Tessaoua-Zinder road which\n\n\ncrosses the eolian sweeping bands can be seen hilly dunes at the\n\nedge of the bands and monticular dunes in the center. The sub\xc2\xad\nstrate is rocky with hardpan phenomena.\n\nThe bands are located\n\n\ndownwind from the Tanout and Koutous massifs. Downwind from the\n\nKoutous Massif which blocks the passage of sand the longer erosion\n\nband reaches a length of 230 km with a width of 25 km. Shorter\n\nerosion bands form below the Tanout Massif, tapering downwind.\n\nDeflation streaks extend the erosion bands of the Koutous and\n\nTanout massifs, north and south of the Koutous Massif and at the\n\nnorthern beginning of the alveolar.\nrules governing their location.\n\n268\n\nWe still donnot understand the\n\n\nHuman occupation begins where\n\n\nthese deflation streaks stop, hence the site 6f the experimental\n\nsquare was well chosen.\n\nWhi\' the erosion bands are unattractive regions for farmers\n\nbecause the rocky substrate crops out in these areas without any\n\nsandy covering, the\n\ndeflation streaks are not unattractive areas\n\n\nas is shown by the region south of Tarka valley west of Dakoro.\n\nB.WWiAid Reactivation: Study of Aerial Photographs\n\n7252\n\n\nNo region, no matter what the dunal relief char\xc2\xad\nacterizing it, seems to be sheltered from current reactivation\n\nphenomena.\n\nHowever, the highest frequency of\n\nthese phenomena\n\n\nis found on the transverse structure which skirts the Koutous\n\nMassif.\n\nThis may explain the concentration of reactivation phe\xc2\xad\n\nnomena in this region.\n\n1. Wind Reactivation and Topographical Factors\n\nWind reactivation often arises downwind from a\n\ntalus, from a trail and from wadidbeds.\n\nWe found it in these\n\n\nplaces on the 1:60,000 scale aerial photographs of 1975 in 2\n\nforms:\n\n(1) in streaks running ENE-WSW obliquely to the wadi beds,\n\n\nalways lcated west of the beds, i.e. downwind from the beds;\n\nor (2) extending\n\nthe end section of the bed of the wadis,\n\n\nbroadening the area affected, which seems to have a tendency to\n\nmove\n\ntowards the west in ring-like or finger-like progressions.\n\na) Reactivation Seems to be promoted by the\n\n\npresence of valleys:\n\n1. for the region extending from 41030 \' \n\nto 150 N and from\n\n6030\' to 7\'50\'E there is a correspondence with the Koren Adwa,\n\nthe Afagag, the Eliki and the Goulbi N\'Tarka.\n\n2. Three small very localized spots\n\nto the south at 13045\n\' ,\n\n\n269\n\n\n13055! and 1405t N and 60 40\'E seem related to the presence of\n\nthe Goulbi N\'Kaba valley and the Goulbi N\'Maradi valley.\n\n0\n3. A~reactivation area located at 14 40N and 80 50\'E is\n\nrelated to the valleys of Dagazaram and Dan Gagara (sources\n\nof the N\'Kaba Goulb!).\n\n4. It tends to\n\nefface the borders of the fields and forms aaind of light film,\n\nlighter than the neighboring areas. Since all of the sand samples have not ,yet reached us we can\n\nonly give partial results in this report. However, we do not\n\nbelieve that the subsequent analyses will invalidate thb results\n\nobtained.\n\n\n273\n\n\nIn the course of two ground trips sand samples were collected\n\n/258\n\nfor the following reasons:\n1. To verify the existence of the large transport current\ntransporting particles from Bilma to Ader Doutchiby showing the\ndecrease in grain size in the direction in which the sand is\nmoved, by showing the existence of load substitution phenomena\nand by showing the degrees of wear on the sandy particles.\n2. To locate the material in the experimental unit along\nthis current.\n3. To specify the degrees of allochthony and/or autochthony\nor the mixtures of distanit and local supplies of sand in the\nsandy material.\n4. To study the existence of a single current or several\nadjacent currents as apparently indicated by the satellite\npictures.\n5. To specify the range of particle sizes of particles\nsubjected to deflation and saltation transport during dunal\nreactivation.\n6. To specify the size of particles according to the type\nof sandy structure which they form.\n7. To investigate differences in grain sizes of the material\naccording to its depth in order to determine variations in\n\nplacement processes, for example superposition of a stratumformed\nby dominant water actions by a~stratum formed by dominant wind\nactions\nThree kinds of laboratory analyses were done on the samples:\n\n(4l\n\n/259\n\n1. Study of grain sizes.\n\n2. Optical study of surface shapes and conditions.\n\n3. Attempt to compare different indices:\n\na) the Qdphi-(of Krumbein) which allows the sorting of\n\nparticles according to the shape of the semi\xc2\xad\nlogarithmic cumulative frequency curve.\nthe Qdphi,\n\nThe greater\n\n\nthe more poorly sorted is the sediment,\n\n\ni.e. heterometric.\n\nb) the mode, which corresponds to the size of particles\n\nof maximum frequency.\n\nc) the median, which is the size of particles such that\n\n50% of the particles are smaller and 50% are larger.\n\nThree groups of samples were distinguished: one group in\n\nthe Termit region, another in the Maradi region and a third\n\nbetween Madaoua and Niamey.\n\nGranulometric Study of Sands ffommthteTermit Region\nA) Comparative Study of Krumbein Qdphi Indices\n\n/261\n\n(Fig. 62).\n\n\nAccording to the value of the Qdphi index three groups can\n\nbe distinguished:\n\n1. Comparative Values of the Mode and Median (Fig. 63)\n\nWe know\n\nthat the difference between the mode and the median\n\n\nreveals the degree of homometry of a mobile formation.\nlarger this difference,\n\nThe\n\n\nthe more poorly sorted, i.e. heterometric,\n\n\nis the formation and vice versa.\n\nFig. 63 enables us to rather quickly evaluate this differencev\n\nLet us draw a bisecting line which connects the angles of the\n\nsquares drawn in: the more distant the points, the greater the\n\nmode-mediat difference. Nevertheless, this distance must be\n\nevaluated in relative terms. In the range of high values a\n\nmoderate difference will correspond to a large difference for the\n\nlow values.\n\nBy using this method -- which is imperfect because it would\n\nbe necessary to precisely define the relativeness described above -\xc2\xad\n\n279\n\n\nwe\n\ncan distinguish several groups:\n\n1. Monticular dunes with:\n\na) a mobile sandy covering which is essential to the\n\nvariety of millet currently being planted within the boundaries\n\nof thb unit.\n\nb) sandy material with a large variety of grain\n\nsizes below the mobile sandy covering. This material is more or\n\nless clastic or solidified. When it crops out as a result of\n\ndeflation it is not donducive to the cultivation of millet.\n\n\n303\n\n\n2. Regions of tabkis or temporary ponds, more abundant in\n\nfine particles, in particular muds, an often supported by a\n\nlayer of lateritic gravels.\n\na) The monticular structures are, above all, the site\n\nof small-scale eolian shifts.\n\nb) The tabkis are the site of water\n\nactivity: light\n\n\nto moderate runoff.\n\nc) Along with "a.\n\nand "b" there is a third mechanism:\n\n\nglazing phenomena in connection with termites. The role of\n\ntermites is important since thby help in the assimilation of\n\nmillet and sorghum straw, but they concentrate the organic residues\n\nand the finest materials by taking them from the soil as a whole.\n\nslight changes introduced in the relatively simple\n\n\nThe\n\nmorphology have to do with soil formation, which in turn is\n\ngoverned by the topography.\n\nThe hydromorphic pseudogley soils\n\n\npertain to the interdunal valleys, and the soils whibh have\n\nbeen slightly solidified as a result of a type of tropical,\n\nferruginous soil formation -- impoverished or leeched soils -\xc2\xad\npertain to the tops and slopes of knolls.\n\n1\n\nBecause of their defficiency in organic matter, their\n\nrelative lack of structure with respect to monticular dunes and\n\nthe poor water dynamics with respect to certain tabkis and inter\xc2\xad\ndunal valleys, all of these soils are unstable: some with\n\nrespect\n\nto wind dynamics, and others with respect to water\n\n\ndynamics.\n\n1. In the chalky Champagne region Renard has shown that the per\xc2\xad\ncentage of organic matter had to be maintained at 4%. Below this\n\nlevel wind and water erosion occur. In Champagne, nearly all\n\nof the beetroot greens and straw and corn stalks, after they have\n\nbeen ground upp are burried in the ground.\n\n\n30~4\n\n\nTwo facts should\n\nbe noted: \n\n\n/288\n\n1. Because of the increasing density of population the\n\noccupation of ground in the experimental unit is nearly at its\n\nmaximum-\n\nMost of the surface area is being used for growing\n\ncereal grains, especially millet and sorghum, and to a slightly\n\nlesser extent corn and\'hiebe."\n\nThe only areas not being cul\xc2\xad\n\ntivated are those which have undergone "glazing,\' and the\n\ntemporary ponds and endoreic basins like tabkis which are\n\nricher in muds and perhaps in clay (examined in the laboratory).\n\nThese last areas are reserved for pasturing; the population\n\ndensity no longer allows ground to be Tet fallow.\n\nThus if\n\n\none wants to continue breeding animals the remaining pasture\n\nland cannot be converted into cultivated ground.\n\n2. In spite of the impossibility of increasing the cultivated\n\nareas and in spite of the decrease in yields experienced by the\n\nfarmers, nevertheless it is necessary to nourish an ever\n\nincreasing population. One way to ease the situation would be\n\nto use plowing. In particular, the fertilizers should be organic\n\nin order to help bind the soil.\n\nMillet tolerates poor soils.\n\n\nThe selection of natural fertilizer sites is based on the\n\ndegree of structural defficinecy of the soils around the villages.\n\nThe problem of working the soil when using plows:\n\nDeep working of the soil is preferable as soon as possible\n\nafter the harvest because:\n\n1. Dirt clogs can thus harden, allowing them to remain\n\nintact and making them less sensitive to deflation.\nth\n\nThis is not\n\n\ncase in a situation where the gcound is sorked dry.\n\n2. The turning under of stubble is also done before the\n\n\n305\n\ntermites can get to it who concentrate organic matter and fine\n\nmineral salts in their nests. On this point, however, opinions\n\nare divided because\n\nit has not been shown that the burying of\n\n\nstubble protectis it from the termites.\n\n\n3\n\n3, Trampling of the ground by animals eating stubble.\n\n\nis avoided.\n\nDiffiulties encountered after the stubble has been turned\n\nunder:\n\n1. In Muslim countries the stubble is the primary material\n\nuseddin the construction of the walls and roofs of huts and field\n\nenclosures. There are no quarries or rocks to supply material for\n\nconstruction and cement is rare and costly.\n\n2. During the dry season the stuble helps fill the gap to\nfeed the animals.\n\nWhen the natives find that the land around\n\n\ntheir villages is breaking down too much, i.e. that eolian\n\nreactivation is becoming too intense, they call in the herds.\n\nThere are manure contracts between animal raisers and farmers,\n\nherds to eat the stubble. The peasant,\n\nfaced with the trampling-plus-natural-mantre option, chooses the\n\n\nthe latter allowing the\nnatural manure.\n\n\nIn 1976 the rainy season had abundant rains which\n\nN.B.:\nlasted into October and broke the record set in 1932. This was\n\nfollowed by a distinct decrease in eolian phenomena such as\n\n"pick-up, transport and deposit in the form of nebkas."\nOnly\n\nsaltation seems\n\nto persist.\n\n\nThe Mobile Sandy Covering\n\nThe mobile sandy covering is extremely important for\n\nthe millet.\n\n306\n\n\nActually\n\nthe seeds are placed at the base of this\n\n\n/289\n\n\ncovering.\n\nIn addition, because of its lack of structure it\n\n\nhelps to better preserve the moisture.\n\nIf the branches of the wind reach the solidified horizon the\n\nsoil becomes less favorable for millet and the natives are forced\n\nto let the ground lie fallow while waiting for the mobile sandy\n\naovering to form again.\n\nThe samples taken by us from the surface and at low depth\n\nin the experimental square and those taken by the soil scientist\n\nC. Feau from holes are in perfect agreement.\n\nOn the whole they\n\n\nall show two princip l modes modes: 160 pm and 315 pm. Sometimes\n\na third mode appears, 100 Vm, but it is much less pronounced.\n\nAnalysis of these samples leads to the following fundamental con\xc2\xad\nclusion: if one compares the sample taken from the surface to\n\nthat taken from a low depth, one finds either an increase in the\n\nmode to 315 pm or a gradual increase from the 315 pm mode to a\n\n400 pm mode. This indicates a relative enrichment of the surface\n\nwith material > 315 pm and proves irrefutably that the finest\n\nmaterial < 315 pm has been carried away. Thus the 315 pm mode\n\nis a threshold with respect to existing eolian transport, iee.\n\nthe reactivation of the sandy mantle. The granulometric curves\n\nshow that the material < 315.Pm can reach and exceed 80%.\n\nExamination of material from hebkas -- small sandy arrows which\n\n\nare formed at certain seasons downwifid from an obstacle (aastalk\n\nof millet, for example) -- produceg the same findings.\n\n\nThe existence in sandy sediments of a supply of coarse\ngrains > 500 pm consisting in part of round, dull grains of\neolian origin is evidence of paleoclimatic winds, which were\nstronger than existing winds and carried in some of the sandy\nmantle in the experimental unit. (With respecttto this pre\xc2\xad\nceding period we are now in a wet phase.)\n\n/290\n\n307\n\n\nHowever, the abundance of fine particles ( 315,.pm), in this\n\nmaterial means that the experimental unit is everywhere vulnerable\n\nto eolian mechanisms.\n\nSwept by the wind, the sand can be\n\n\ndeposited on the top of the highest monticular dtnes, trapped by\n\nthe hedges which rise up or yet a-ain by the villages which\n\ncreate obstable effects.\n\n(This explains the striking fact of the\n\n\nfreshness, homcmetry and the quality of sand in the villages;\n\nduring each dry season it is actually renewed by eolian trans\xc2\xad\nport.)\n\nAs we have already said, vulnerability to water erosion\n\nrepresents a relatively small, non-threatening danger. The only\n\nregions affected by runoff are located around endorheic basins\n\nand ponds.\n\nThe 1:20,000 scale map of the experimental unit that we\n\nhave drawn up shows how much the entire experimental unit has\n\nbecome unstable. This is still very difficult to estimate at\n\nthe present time, however, it is a real phenomenon.\n\nThis map\n\n\nalso shows the overwhelming extent of grounds occupied by crops\n\nand the small amount of area left fallow.\n\nThe farmers are drawing\n\n\nattention to a drop in production which they attribute to the\n\nlack of fallow ground.\n\nNearly everywhere there are signs of reactivation of the\n\nsandy mantle, as is especially brought to light by the study of\n\nthe aerial photographs obtianed during the special mission.\n\nThese signs are less prominent in the rings of ground fertilized\n\nwith animal manure around the villages.\nholding firm,\n\nbut their existnee constitutes a warning bell!\n\n\nfor future planners, since\nirreversible traumas.\n\n\n308\n\nThese areas are still\n\n\nimproper managemeht could cause\n\n\ntG1-og6aidryf Scientific and Technical Terms tUsed\' \n\n\n/291\n\nAblation: Removal of material. It may be due to the potential vulnerability of\n\nthe natural environment, to the vulnerability caused by an in\xc2\xad\ncrease in external dynamic mechanisms due to climatic fluctuations\n\nand to the instability brought on by man made pressures.\n\nGhourd: Predominant pyramidal dune from whibh silks radiate\n\nin any direction whatsoever. The two\n\nare a momentaneous expression of a dynamic situation: external\n\nfor the surface relief dnd internal for the relief. The surface\n\n\n314\n\nrelief is a temporary state of equilibrium due to the action\n\non the internal relief by the climate and mechanisms which derive\n\nfrom it.\n\nMorphogenesis or glyptogenesis: Sequence of phenomena\n\nwhich form the landscapes.\n\nMprphology: Sci~Bce which studies-morphogenetic mechanisms.\n\nMorphescopy: Study of the shape and surface conditions of\n\ngrains of sand.\n\nMonsoon: Change in the direction of the zonal flow of trade\n\nwiids when they cross the equator.\n\nSandy "tablecloth": Thin, continuous sandy covering.\n\nNebka, micronebka: Accumulation of sand downwind from a\n\nplant. Thb pl&nt can rise up when the accumulation increases.\n\nNebkas can reach several meters in height and can be 10-20\n\nmeters in diameter.\n\nStationary waves: Produced by the superposition of pro\xc2\xad\ncessions of infrasonic waves such that a periodic phenomenon\n\nresults involving an alternation of nodes where the osciiations\n\nare in opposite pahse and the movement minimum, and involving\n\nbulges, when the waves are in phase,\n\nwith maximum displacement.\n\n\nGiant uniform wave: Linear sandy structure, with a convex\n\ntransverse profile, sometimes covered with\n\na pavement, and\n\n\nmade uniform by eolian deflation and winnowing.\n\nEolian pavement: Coarse particles left behind by deflation\n\nwhich covereeolian sandy structures. Thbse particles are too\n\nlarge to be moved by the force of existing effective winds.\n\n\n315\n\nPedogenesis: All the biologica and physicochemical trans\xc2\xad\nformations undergone by a rock during its conversion into soil.\n\n,___Impact film: Highly impermeable film formed on the surface\n\nof a loose soil by the impact of water drops.\n\nSolidification: Slightlaggregation caused by an abundance of\n\noxide and/or clay.\n\nGullying: Rain erosion due to concentrated runoff.\n\nReg: Eolian deflation pavement.\n\n/297\n\n\nReactivation: Phenomenon of moving a moveable material,\n\nformerly fixed by a blanket of vegetation or by a horizon\n\nenrighed by organic matter or by a deflation pavement.\n\nRipple marks: Surface state of a sandy mantle with asymmetric\n\nripples without an active crest. They have a gentle slope\n\nfacing the wind and a steeper slope facing downwind. In the case\n\nof a ripple the main transport mechanism is creep or rolling.\n\nRose diagrams: Graphic representation of the frequency and\n\nvelocity of winds according to their direction.\n\nDiffuse
